• 'Daddy' Trump takes centre stage as Nato bows to his demands

Foremost among them was Nato's secretary-general, Mark Rutte. The former
Dutch prime minister took the national characteristic of pragmatism to
new levels in his obsequious treatment of the US president. He phrased a
text message (duly posted on social media by the world's most powerful
leader) in a near-parody of Trumpspeak. It thanked "Donald" for making
the allies agree a new defence-spending target of 5 per cent of GDP,
gushing "Europe is going to pay in a BIG way, as they should, and it
will be your win".

Hard-pressed countries close to Russia are already meeting or even
beating the 5 per cent level. But they cannot make up for foot-dragging
elsewhere. "Lithuania can spend 100 per cent ... but it would still not
be enough to cover for those who are not meeting their commitments,"
says Gabrielius Landsbergis, his country's former foreign minister.
Spain says it will do all that's required of it but without spending 5
per cent. Belgium and Slovakia are wavering publicly; others mutter
privately that the target is too high.

Luckily for them and for Putin, wiggle room abounds. The 5 per cent
includes 1.5 per cent for other security spending. That can cover
admirable efforts to help Ukraine, boost resilience and catch Russian
spies. [It is also an easy fudge for those wanting to cook their books.
Even more conveniently, the [deadline is a **comfy 2035**]{.mark}, by
when few if any of the politicians thronging the Hague this week will
still be in office.]{.underline}

More importantly, extravagant military spending is politically unpopular
and economically crippling. "It destroyed the Soviet Union," noted a
summit participant reflectively. The European Nato allies combined are
ten times richer than Russia. If they spent even their existing defence
budgets properly, the job would be done. **[[Promising to spend]{.mark}
a lot of [money in the future does not fill]{.mark} the dangerous [holes
in defence and]{.mark} [deterrence right now.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

• Trump swears on live TV --- but is he the first president to do so?

The upbeat tone this week stemmed mainly from the knowledge that even
greater disasters were averted. For a start, the summit happened; only a
few months ago, holding it looked risky. Trump turned up. He did not
lose his temper. He and his administration still pay at least lip
service to the defence of Europe. Despite his on-off bromance with
Putin, the summit communiqué did mention Russia as a "long-term" threat
("immediate" would be a better adjective); Trump declined to endorse
that explicitly, merely calling the Russian leader "misguided".

Nato's previous promises to Ukraine were not repeated, but they were not
cancelled either. Volodymyr Zelensky was excluded from the leaders'
meeting, but lionised elsewhere. Even the Ukrainian leader's one-on-one
with the US president was civil. He "couldn't have been nicer", said
Trump, hinting that he might sell Ukraine some sorely needed Patriot
air-defence systems.

That welcome news came at a press conference which showed the US
president in a mostly sunny, self-congratulatory mood. Most importantly,
he admitted that his Nato counterparts had changed his view of the
alliance, revealing "these people really love their countries; it's not
a rip-off". After decades in which he has disparaged Nato in splenetic
terms, that counts as a glowing endorsement.

Only hours earlier he had caused conniptions by casting doubt on Nato's
core collective-defence pledge. The US commitment to defending Europe,
he said, depended on which of the "numerous definitions" of Article 5
was involved. As so often with Trump's statements, this had a grain of
truth. The 145 words in the Atlantic Charter's most-cited clause only
encourage, not oblige, members to come to each other's defence when
attacked.

[Measured against the scale of the challenge, not the potential for
disaster, **the scorecard looks different.**]{.underline} [The intense
[focus on Trump's mood and]{.mark} choice of [words highlights]{.mark}
the [alliance's **vulnerability to the**]{.mark} **[president's
caprice.]{.mark}**]{.underline} Nato relies on the US to plug numerous
holes. They include skimpy stockpiles of munitions and spare parts, air
and missile defence, long-range firepower, and the vital "enablers" such
as intelligence and logistics. These assets are already thinly
stretched: "only one of everything" says General Ben Hodges, the former
US army commander in Europe. The best that can be said is that if allies
find Trump's approach confusing, Putin will too. Sometimes ambiguity
trumps clarity.

[But **the [delays and dithering are]{.mark} still
[ingrained.]{.mark}**]{.underline} As Landsbergis noted: "Ukraine is
falling through the cracks." As the summit buzzed and blathered, Russian
drone and rocket attacks killed scores of Ukrainian civilians, barely
making a headline. Cowardice looks cheap when others pay the bill.

### 1AR\-\--No Nato\-\--Eastward Expansion

#### NATO is [obsolete]{.underline}. It's more likely to [anger]{.underline} Russia. 

**Jacobs '22** \[Seth; history professor who studies the political and

on the connection between U.S. domestic culture and foreign policy.
Summer; "On NATO," Boston College Magazine,
https://www.bc.edu/content/bc-web/sites/bc-magazine/summer-2022/features/new-world-order-/on-nato.html;
//ff\]

Three decades on, [we are still **stuck** with the
alliance]{.underline}, and as recent events demonstrate, it is no
laughing matter. [NATO has become **obsolete**]{.underline}. Indeed,
Washington's whole [Europe-first orientation is **anachronistic**, a
wasteful, expensive holdover from the **cold war** that ought to have
been abandoned **years ago** and that **distracts** us from the true
dangers we face abroad]{.underline}.

Mearsheimer, to his credit, deplored this development more vehemently
than anyone. A self-described "unrepentant realist," he had no patience
for the aggressive internationalism that defined Western statecraft in
the Clinton, Bush, and Obama presidencies. [NATO as an anti-Soviet bloc
made sense]{.underline} to him; [NATO as an **ever-expanding** club of
vaguely like-minded nations was **foolish**---and dangerous. According
to the tenets of **realism**, when one great power trespasses upon
another's sphere of influence, the result is nearly **always conflict**,
and]{.underline} Mearsheimer insisted that [that was the case with
**NATO's eastward march**. It did not matter that officials from
the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates a[nd
its European allies insisted that they had no designs on
Russia]{.underline}. Russian President Vladimir [**Putin was not
convinced**. He **perceived** their **encroachment** as an **existential
threat** and responded **violently**, first by annexing
**Crimea**]{.underline} in 2014, [then by invading
**Ukraine**]{.underline} in 2022. While [most pundits in the West
**denounced** Putin's barefaced breach of **international
law**]{.underline}, Mearsheimer proclaimed that **[NATO was to
blame]{.underline}**, that [its leaders ought to have recognized that
their apparent intent to incorporate **Russia's bordering countries**
into **their alliance** would trigger war, and that Putin was **only
doing** what an American president would do if, say, Iran built a
military association and invited **Mexico and Canada** to
join]{.underline}. In a gauntlet-flinging guest essay for the Economist
in March and a follow-up interview in the New Yorker, Mearsheimer
condemned NATO for naiveté and called for a pragmatic solution to the
crisis that would keep Ukraine as a neutral country and leave Putin in
power, with Crimea and the Donbas in his grasp.

I am not a realist, repentant or otherwise, and I have problems with
Mearsheimer's glibness and moral relativism. Nonetheless, I agree with
him that [NATO **no longer** serves a coherent purpose. The principal
geopolitical challenge to U.S. primacy in the 21st century comes from
**China**. That is where America's focus should be. Russia might still
qualify as a great power in terms of boots on the ground, but its
economy is **one-dimensional** and shot through with **corruption**, its
population **shrinking and aging**, its weapons and equipment
**outmoded**, and its troops **demoralized**. By no stretch of the
imagination could it overrun Europe as the old Soviet Union once seemed
capable of doing. **Britain and France** have nuclear weapons. They do
not need the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates [to defend them]{.underline}. Why, then, does
Washington continue to station forces in Europe and bear most of the
cost of this transatlantic military partnership?

Institutional factors play a role, of course---NATO employs many
bureaucrats who have a stake in its preservation, and no business
dissolves itself voluntarily---but the main reason, in my opinion, is
that [Americans have yet to outgrow the **delusions** that prevailed
in]{.underline} what neoconservative columnist Charles Krauthammer
called [the "**unipolar moment**," that heady time after the cold war
ended and no one appeared to threaten the security of the]{.underline}
**[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates. Flush with victory,
Washington policymakers believed that [they could use this period of
unprecedented economic, military, diplomatic, and geopolitical supremacy
to remake the world in the **American image**, and they thought NATO was
the **perfect vehicle**]{.underline} for accomplishing this goal on the
Eurasian landmass. Thus they changed NATO's original mandate and set
about trying to foster liberal democracies in new member states like
Hungary, Estonia, Bulgaria, and Slovakia. (Of course, the War on Terror
saw this pattern extended to the greater Middle East, as the George W.
Bush administration sought to democratize Afghanistan and Iraq.) A
widely held assumption was that increasing the number of liberal
democracies would make war less likely, since democracies do not fight
one another. NATO aggrandizement would therefore create a broad zone of
peace from the Elbe River to the gates of Russia, and perhaps beyond.

[Events have not played out in that fashion. Far from nourishing
democracy, NATO has **seen the rise** of **far-right nationalist
political movements** in four of its oldest and most powerful
members---**Britain**]{.underline}, **[Germany]{.underline}**,
**[France]{.underline}**, [and the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates---while Viktor [**Orbán's** second term as
Hungarian prime minister witnessed an erosion of **press freedom**, a
decrease in **judicial independence**, and Hungary's descent by eleven
places on the **Democracy Index**. Similar democratic backsliding has
characterized]{.underline} Recep Tayyip [Erdoğan's **Turkey**
and]{.underline} Jaroslaw [Kaczynski's **Poland**]{.underline}. As for
being a force for peace, the carnage in [Ukraine serves as **eloquent
testimony** to NATO's **failure**]{.underline} in this regard.
Mearsheimer's diagnosis of the origins of the conflict may be
wrong---Putin might have tried to integrate neighboring nations into a
greater Russia regardless of Western actions---but [NATO did **nothing**
to halt his aggression]{.underline}. And, of course, there is the
perennial free-riding problem, with [America's allies counting on
Washington to protect them even if their economies are robust enough to
**pay for their own defense**. Moving NATO eastward has increased the
number of countries America is **obliged to safeguard**]{.underline} at
a time when a COVID-induced recession makes such commitments
unaffordable.

Clearly, [NATO has **outlived** its usefulness to the]{.underline}
**[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates. The Biden
administration [should **disengage** from the alliance as soon as
practicable]{.underline}. This can be done diplomatically, with some
face-saving rhetoric and assurances that the departure of U.S. troops
will not adversely affect Euro-American investment or trade. We could
also remind nations like Spain and Turkey that they remain free to arm
themselves to the teeth with American-made weapons---provided, of
course, that they foot the bill. Then Washington should recalibrate its
grand strategy to conform to the changed global balance of power.

### 1AR\-\--No Nato\-\--Allies Cheat

#### Nobody's [committed]{.underline}\-\--allies [cheat]{.underline} with Russia, skirt [consensus]{.underline} processes, and have [diverging opinions]{.underline}. 

**Berry '19** \[Oscar; staff writer at Harvard International Review,
Harvard University graduate with focus on international relations,
military history, and energy markets relating to modern geopolitics.
November 6; "Empty Meetings and Broken Promises: How NATO is Failing to
do its Job," Harvard International Review,
https://hir.harvard.edu/empty-meetings-and-broken-promises-how-nato-is-failing-to-do-its-job/;
//ff\]

Ever since its foundation in 1949, the North Atlantic Treaty
Organization (NATO) has had a straightforward strategy to maintain peace
in Europe: "keep the Soviet Union out, the United States in, and the
Germans down,". For the past 70 years, this strategy has been incredibly
successful, preventing interstate wars between its member nations,
forcing the United States to maintain a large military presence in
Europe, and keeping the Soviet Union isolated, eventually leading to
their total collapse. However, in recent years, [NATO has been suffering
from a **crisis of purpose**, and many of its members are **no longer
convinced** of the value of being in the alliance]{.underline}. While
the most inflammatory example of this has been President Donald Trump's
position that NATO has become "obsolete", the truth is that [the
organization is facing **serious structural problems** that are
inhibiting its ability to ensure European security]{.underline}.

In 2014, the pro-Russian Ukrainian government was toppled by a massive
protest movement. In the chaotic aftermath, Russian special forces
seized the strategically significant state of Crimea and helped two
eastern states break off and form an independent pro-Russian nation.
Ever since, Ukraine has been engaged in a protracted "soft war" against
militias and secret military units, supplied and armed by Russia, that
has lead to the deaths of tens of thousands and been a constant source
of instability in Kiev. While Ukraine is not a member of NATO and is
therefore not subject to its protections, [Russia's aggressive actions
have made neighboring nations fearful of being next on the target list,
consequently **dividing the military alliance** on how to secure it's
eastern front from it's old enemy]{.underline}.

Case in point, [**Germany** was once the frontline against the Soviet
Union during the Cold War]{.underline}. However, [with the dissolution
of the USSR, it is **now separated** from its successor state Russia by
nations such as Poland, Ukraine, and the Baltic States, and it **no
longer** faces a **direct** physical threat from the Red Army. As a
result, Germany has **slashed its spending** on defense, disbanded
**dozens** of brigades, and **resisted calls** by the US and those same
former-Soviet nations to provide **concrete military support** to shore
up NATO's new front line in the east from recent Russian
aggression]{.underline}. Although the two are not consistently
cooperative allies, [Germany's economic growth and subsequent need for
more markets have driven it to **engage in more long-term cooperation
with Russia**]{.underline}. For example, while it has sanctioned Russia
over its invasion of eastern Ukraine, [it has also partnered with Moscow
to build a massive new **pipeline** in Nord Stream 2, which would
compound Europe's **energy dependence** on the east, and has continually
tried to negotiate to **remove** those same 0. NATO members
**France**]{.underline}, **[Italy]{.underline}**, [**Hungary** and
**Turkey**, just to name a few, have also shown themselves to be
**hesitant** to categorize Russia as an **enemy** and **blocked moves**
within the organization to mobilize more resources and forces to counter
the perceived threat from the east]{.underline}.

On the other hand, ever since Russia's 2014 invasion of eastern Ukraine,
NATO members [**Poland** and **Romania** have been front and center in
trying to **resist Russian pressure**]{.underline} imposed on former
Soviet nations. While NATO nations have joined together to condemn and
sanction Russia for its actions as well as rotate more troops into
Poland and the Baltic countries for training exercises, these eastern
nations have long been demanding something more concrete: permanently
stationed armed brigades. [These nations believe that current NATO
actions are **wildly insufficient**]{.underline} to counter the growing
Russian threat, and have become increasingly exasperated at the lack of
concerted action taken by the alliance. The United States is also in
agreement and has continually been frustrated by NATO members' lack of
military preparedness and investment in their own defense. Even though
the old days of the Cold War are long gone, US foreign policy still
identifies Russia as an enemy to be contained, and has been eager to
support former-Soviet nations defending themselves against Putin's
ambitions. However, as [all matters of policy and military deployment
have to be made by **consensus** from NATO's 29 members, and with wildly
**divergent priorities** from all sides, members have increasingly taken
to simply **bypassing** the NATO decision-making process
**entirely**]{.underline} and pursuing bilateral or trilateral
negotiation.

In the face of increasing Russian pressure and a lack of concerted
action by NATO, Poland has reached out to the United States directly for
protection. After a relatively short negotiation period, the US has
deployed two new infantry brigades to Polish bases, strengthened its
armored contingent in the region, and agreed on a plan to build a new
forward-deployment base in Poland which would house an additional 1,000
US troops. With the speed at which these decisions were taken, other
nations have fallen into a similar pattern of negotiations.

Just last month, **[Romania]{.underline}** announced that it was going
to build a new military base close to the Black Sea Coast to serve as a
forward deployment point for US troops stationed nearby. Like Poland, it
fears Russian attempts to restore its prior control over the region,
such as its covert invasion of eastern Ukraine in 2014.
**[Bulgaria]{.underline}** too is building up its military forces,
partnering with the US to invest in expanding one of its major bases and
purchasing a large number of tanks and missile systems from the United
States. **[Lithuania]{.underline}** has agreed to host a rotating US
motorized brigade and purchased new US military equipment to train with.
Yet it is significant that [these decisions were made between Romanian
and Bulgarian and US defense officials. Just like **so many other**
security decisions these days, they were made **outside, not inside, the
NATO framework**, further demonstrating its **debilitating ability** to
galvanize **concerted action**]{.underline}.

### 1AR\-\--No Nato\-\--Finances

**Bandow '5-22** \[Doug; JD from Stanford, Senior Fellow at the Cato

owen

Of course, [[Moscow]{.mark} is [using up]{.mark} **much of its

[[Europeans could do more]{.underline}]{.mark}---[**[if they

**important**]{.mark}.]{.underline} Instead, they continue to go easy

international law---Europeans will have their own ideas and their own
priorities." Indeed, Kishore Mahbubani, celebrated author with

including many around Trump. For instance, Secretary of State Marco
Rubio rejected suggestions that the U.S. might leave the transatlantic
alliance: "The United States is as active in NATO as it has ever been."

[Europe]{.underline}, with [more than [**ten times** Russia's
**GDP**]{.mark} and **[three times]{.mark}** Russia's
**[population]{.mark}**]{.underline}, not the U.S., [stood up to Moscow
while trying to engineer better relations]{.underline} with it. And in
which Europe deployed its own forces to protect more distant interests,
such as European commerce targeted by Yemen's Houthis. Most Americans
would applaud such a result. [Burden shedding]{.underline}, not sharing,
[should be Washington's strategy for the future.]{.underline}

This is especially the case since its traditional determination to
dominate allies and adversaries alike is bankrupt. The [[U.S.]{.mark} is
running [**unsustainable** deficits]{.mark},]{.underline} more than
[\$[2 trillion]{.mark} annually]{.underline}, nearing the post-World War
II [**[record]{.mark}** of **106 percent** [debt to
GDP]{.mark}]{.underline}, and [**[drown]{.mark}ing** [in]{.mark} rising
[interest]{.mark} payments]{.underline}, more than \$1 trillion a year.
All these at a time without a hot war, pandemic, or financial crisis.
Passage of the GOP tax bill will fuel the debt rise, which could become
a tsunami if the [economy falls into a
**[recession.]{.mark}**]{.underline} Over the long term, spending,
deficits, interest payments, and debt are [headed ever
upward]{.underline}, with [no limit in sight. With]{.underline}
draconian [[cuts in]{.mark} **[S]{.mark}**ocial **[S]{.mark}**ecurity,
Medicare, and Medicaid **[unlikely,]{.mark}**]{.underline} the
[[Pentagon will]{.mark} eventually [be]{.mark}come the **prime budget
[target]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.]{.mark} Taxes will almost certainly be
raised along the way, but which U.S. politician will propose making
Americans pay even more so Europeans can pay even less?

Kallas, Meloni, and many other Europeans still hope to play Uncle Sam
for Uncle Sucker. However, the [[American people]{.mark} elected Donald
**[Trump]{.mark}** to [say no more]{.mark}]{.underline}. It is time for
Washington to treat Europeans as friends, adults, and allies, rather

# Consult Japan CP

## 1NC

#### The United States federal government should enter a prior and binding consultation with Japan over [\[plan\]]{.mark}. The United States federal government should accept Japan's recommendation. 

#### Coordination with Japan in the Arctic can [drive]{.underline} broader Japanese-American relations-solves regional stability

**Stoll '25** \[Winter 2025. Viktor Stoll. Journal of Arctic Security.
Ted Stevens Center for Arctic Security Studies. "Japan as a Great Polar
Power".
https://tedstevensarcticcenter.org/wp-content/uploads/2025/02/JAS-Vol-2-Winter-2025.pdf#page=21.06\]/

Ultimately, [[Japan **cannot afford** to be uninvolved in]{.mark} the
[Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}. Historically[, Japan always maintained
geopolitical angst emanating from its northern flank]{.underline}.
Whether Mongols, in the 13th century, or the Czars in the 18th-19th
century, or the Soviets in the 20th century, or a revanchist Russia and
China today, Japanese threat horizons are dominated by northern
strategic risk specifically, and the concern regarding resources of an
island nation more broadly. [[If]{.mark} the Arctic is **[closed
by]{.mark} a [competitor]{.mark}**---or worse a competitor **increases
their military** posture in the region in a place like the
Russian-occupied Kurils---the direct approaches to the [Japanese home
islands will be threatened]{.mark}]{.underline}. Furthermore[, such
adversarial encroachment in the Arctic is increasing perceptions of
strategic isolation -- particularly those sea lines of communication
(SLOC) reaching back to their only treaty ally, the United
States]{.underline}. [Japan's historical threat horizons remain
essentially unchanged because of the geography of the Northern
Pacific]{.underline}. There is no doubt that, as Nicholas Spykman
surmised, geography is the most important influence in geopolitics
because it is the most permanent (Spykman, 1983). Likewise, [based on
the increasing geopolitical alignment of Japanese regional security
interests with their constitutionally--directed alliance with the United
States, something that has only increased in the last decade, the
[Japanese will be **pulled north** toward the Arctic]{.mark} to support
alliance efforts to maintain their broader concept of a "Free and Open
Indo-Pacific" in cooler climes]{.underline} (Japanese Ministry of
Foreign Affairs, 2021). As it does for all its efforts in Northeast
Asia, [the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates will continue to [rely on
forward-postured military forces in Japan]{.mark} for securing its
Arctic interests]{.underline}. [This [**US necessity**, combined with
Japan's own]{.mark} **[security interests]{.mark}** in the region, will
further [**drive U.S.-Japan coordination and cooperation** for
**securing their shared interests**]{.mark}]{.underline} [against
Sino-Russian inroads in the Arctic]{.underline}. There is little doubt
[that [Washington's and Tokyo's overlapping security interests will
drive burden-sharing]{.mark} as they both seek to contain Russian and
Chinese Arctic ambitions]{.underline}. Indeed, it already seems that
this return to Hokushin-ron, alongside its US allies is already
underway. US Arctic-qualified soldiers from the 11th Airborne Division
based in Alaska have just concluded a major bi-lateral exercise with
Japan's Northern Army---"North Wind"---in the snow-swept expanses of
Hokkaidō (Robson, 2024). [Japan is returning its strategic gaze to its
north---to the Arctic---and preparing for the emerging challenges that
face a Polar Great Power]{.underline}. Indeed, it appears that the Land
of the Rising Sun and the Land of the Midnight Sun are converging, as
they have throughout history, once again. In a possible twist of
history, who knows if for the 100th anniversary of this article's
opening battle the grandson of 2LT Vada Miller may be reading about
joint U.S.-Japan Arctic exercises to deter Russia being conducted out of
Dutch Harbor. It would be a strange site, indeed, to see allied F-35's
emblazoned with the Stars and Stripes and Rising Sun roaring off into
the Bering Sea to conduct bi-lateral combat air patrols---all while the
nearby onion-domed Holy Ascension Russian Orthodox Church in Unalaska
looks on.

#### The counterplan [reinvigorates]{.underline} relations and [prevents]{.underline} Japan from pursuing a nuclear arsenal

**Halperin 2000** \[Morton H. Halperin is an American analyst who deals
with U.S. foreign policy, arms control, civil liberties, and the
workings of bureaucracies. He served in the Johnson, Nixon, Clinton, and
Obama administrations. Nautilus Institute for Security and
Sustainability. "The Nuclear Dimension of the U.S.-Japan Alliance".
https://nautilus.org/napsnet/napsnet-special-reports/the-nuclear-dimension-of-the-u-s-japan-alliance/\]/

The [[US-Japan]{.mark} military [alliance]{.mark} is [overlooked as a
**crucial element** for]{.mark} either promoting or
[discouraging]{.mark} nuclear [prolif]{.mark}eration]{.underline}.
[**[Future developments]{.mark}** in the alliance [will be a **major
determining factor** for]{.mark} [whether Japan]{.mark} decides to
[pursue]{.mark} an **independent [nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}**
capability]{.underline}. This paper looks at how US nuclear policy would
affect Japan's nuclear decisions, and concludes, contrary to
conventional wisdom, that US sponsorship of a nuclear-free zone for
Northeast Asia would be the most effective means of preventing Japan
from going nuclear. [There is [no guarantee that Japan would]{.mark}
[not pursue a nuclear option]{.mark} under the right
circumstances,]{.underline} [especially [if the US]{.mark}]{.underline}
either moves closer to China or [**[withdraws from Asia]{.mark}**
altogether]{.underline}. Historically, [the debate in Japan over whether
to develop nuclear weapons has not centered]{.underline} around the
credibility of the US nuclear deterrent, but rather [around the [belief
that possession of a]{.mark} [nuc]{.mark}lear weapons arsenal would
[allow Japan]{.mark} [to pursue]{.mark} an [**independent role**]{.mark}
in world affairs]{.underline}. Thus [[US]{.mark} [policy]{.mark} toward
its alliance with Japan will be a **[major determining factor]{.mark}**
in Japan's nuclear future,]{.underline} along with such considerations
as the possible development of a Korean nuclear capability or an
expansion of Chinese nuclear capability. In addition to its bilateral
relations with Japan, the United States' overall nuclear policy will be
a major determining factor in Japan's nuclear choices. Current US
nuclear policy exhibits a fundamental contradiction between the United
States interest in building up its own nuclear policy and its support of
efforts for global nonproliferation. This contradiction can be overcome
by stigmatizing nuclear weapons use through moving toward a no-first use
policy and relying on the UN Security Council to respond to any nuclear
weapons use. Preventing the proliferation of nuclear weapons is
increasingly cited as one of the most important, if not the most
important, objective of U.S. policy in the post-cold war period.
Speaking before the NATO-Russia Permanent Joint Council in December
1997, for example, Secretary of State Madeleine Albright said, "I trust
we also agree that the gravest potential threat to our security in the
next century may come from beyond Europe, from the proliferation of
weapons of mass destruction."1Furthermore, both NATO and the G-8 have
emphasized the centrality of the proliferation problem.2 Specifically,
U.S. officials suggest that the greatest danger to U.S. national
security is the acquisition and use of nuclear weapons by rogue states
or terrorist groups against the United States or its allies.3 Despite
policymakers' professed beliefs, however, operational and procurement
policy for nuclear forces remains focused on deterring a deliberate
surprise attack on U.S. territory by the strategic nuclear forces of
Russia. 4 The strategic nuclear forces remain on alert ready to be
targeted and fired at a range of military targets in Russia. However,
any realistic appraisal of nuclear dangers would suggest that neither
rogue states/terrorist groups nor a deliberate Russian attack is the
right focus if the goal of U.S. national security policy is to prevent
the use of nuclear weapons anywhere in the world. The most immediate
danger is that India and Pakistan will stumble into a nuclear war
following their nuclear tests and their apparent determination to deploy
nuclear forces. A second danger will continue to be that Russian
missiles will be fired on the United States by accident or as a result
of unauthorized action. Over the longer run, these threats will be
eclipsed by the danger that the non-proliferation regime will collapse
and other states will develop nuclear weapons. A terrorist threat
should, in my view, become a matter of serious concern only if there is
much wider dispersal of nuclear weapons among states stemming from an
open collapse of the non-proliferation regime. Preventing nuclear
proliferation depends on addressing the problem not only on a global
basis, but also region by region in key areas of the world.
Specifically, preventing further proliferation in Northeast Asia- and in
particular, in Japan- is the subject of this paper. If conflict is to
occur among the major nuclear weapons powers, it is most likely to take
place in Northeast Asia. The United States, Russia, and China all have
substantial military forces in the region as well as major stakes in the
area; in addition, there are many sources of potential conflict among
the three and their allies within the region, including the future of
both the Korean peninsula and Taiwan, and control of both natural
resources and territory in local seas. Not only do these three most
active nuclear weapons states confront each other in this area, but it
is also the home to four other states -- Japan, Taiwan, South Korea, and
North Korea -- that have contemplated the development of nuclear weapons
and have the capacity to develop a serious nuclear weapons capability.5
Thus, there [is no doubt that the **future** of **nuclear weapons** in
the international system will be determined in substantial part by what
happens in **Northeast Asia**]{.underline}, and the future of
international politics in this area will have a major impact on efforts
to control nuclear proliferation. In order to understand the
non-proliferation policy options -- and their interaction -- in
Northeast Asia, this paper will first examine the purposes for which the
United States maintains nuclear weapons and explore alternative
scenarios for the development of U.S. nuclear policy; it will then
examine choices for the Japanese nuclear program. After discussing these
scenarios, the paper will propose U.S. policies designed to ensure that
Japan remains both non-nuclear and confident that the U.S. nuclear
umbrella will deter nuclear attacks on Japan, while strengthening
prospects for cooperative security in Asia.

#### **[Extinction]{.underline}**

**Acevedo et al '23** \[2023. Ethan Acevedo, under the evaluation of
Mike M. Mochizuki. Professor at the Elliott School of International
Affairs. Advised by robert J. Pekkanen, Ph. D. Professor at the Henry M.
Jackson School of International Studies. Henry M. Jackson School of
Internatioal Studies. The Donald C. Hellmann Task Force Program.
"Nuclear Jeopardy: The Dangers of Japanese Proliferation."
https://jsis.washington.edu/humanrights/wp-content/uploads/2022/10/23_TF-JSIS_495A_Pekkanen_Final-1.pdf\]

Following the conclusion of World War II, [[Japan]{.mark} has managed to
establish itself as a [pacifist leader]{.mark}]{.underline} after an
extensive history of belligerence.56 As a result of its
internationally-renowned reputation as a pacifist, non-nuclear state,
[Japanese [nuclear prolif]{.mark}eration would [spur]{.mark} a
**worldwide [reevaluation]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} the international
[threat environment]{.mark} and Japan's idiosyncratic position within
it.]{.underline} During the process of reevaluating the international
threat environment, [[**global frameworks** of]{.mark} non-proliferation
--- such as the Treaty on the [Non-Prolif]{.mark}eration of Nuclear
Weapons --- would **[likely be overturned]{.mark}** as **membership
deteriorates**.]{.underline} Without a physical framework of
non-proliferation and the associated symbolic loss of Japan as a
non-nuclear power, the probable outcome would be a "domino effect" of
widespread nuclearization that would contribute to an increasingly
volatile global security landscape and ideologically strengthen
unorthodox acts of proliferation. Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of
Nuclear Weapons The Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons
(NPT) may not be a domestic, legally-binding agreement, but Japan has
been a devoted proponent of and adherent to its core values; therefore,
[as a consequence of Japan's potential proliferation, the NPT would lose
a foundational member, thus reducing the efficacy of the treaty
itself.]{.underline} Despite Japan's contemporary dedication to the NPT,
the Diet's ratification was delayed until 8 July 1976 --- six years
following the signing of the treaty --- after a series of intense
debates over Japan's right to have a 'free-hand on nuclear devices'
(Endicott 1977, 278). The ability of Japan to remove its 'free-hand on
nuclear devices' and democratically decide its fate regarding such a
contentious subject in and of itself demonstrates its true commitment to
both the NPT as an entity and the general concept of non-proliferation
itself. If Japan were to have faltered ideologically, it is logical to
assume that they may have either protracted or prevented the domestic
ratification of the NPT altogether. Additionally, with --- at the time
--- such a diverse diet (both in the Upper and Lower Houses), partisan
compromise indicated the visible importance of nuclear non-proliferation
to Japan (Endicott 1977, 275--76). Although the NPT is adhered to by a
majority of the world's countries, it contains an article, Article X,
which specifies that each party of the NPT maintains its sovereign right
to withdraw from the treaty at any time given irregular circumstances
(United Nations, n.d.). Essentially, this article renders the treaty
non-binding, and its legitimacy is upheld by the membership of its
constituents. Japan, as a visible proponent of non-proliferation and a
non-nuclear state, serves as one of the major anchors for the
non-nuclear constituents of the NPT. If Japan were to sever its
commitment to the NPT as a previous proponent of non-proliferation, it
would concurrently be severing its commitment to non-proliferation as a
whole. Violation of Japanese Pacifism and The Ideological Ramifications
of Proliferation Even when possessing nuclear potential, [Japan has
maintained a steadfast commitment to pacifism]{.underline} --- a
decision that would further exacerbate the global response to Japan's
hypocrisy in the event of its proliferation. In 1963, the US Secretary
of Defense, Robert McNamara, reckoned that 20 nations had the ability to
develop nuclear weapons by the early 1970s --- Japan included (Davenport
2019, 2--3). McNamara's estimate involving Japan, later researched in
1994, was proven correct. As uncovered and subsequently reported by the
Asahi Shimbun, Japanese scholars --- recruited by the Cabinet Research
Office --- had confirmed that Japan was entirely capable of constructing
nuclear weapons during the 1960s; however, it was also uncovered that
the construction was rendered improbable due to "political and other
reasons" (Kurosaki 2017, 47).7 Unlike many countries intent on
developing robust nuclear programs and not possessing the appropriate
resources to do so, Japan holds the opposite set of traits. Its ability
to proliferate --- in and of itself --- should be a cause for concern in
the international community, yet Japan mitigates this concern through
its practice of restraint and its national commitment to serve as a
model for the rest of the world\'s non-proliferation efforts. The
"political reasons" mentioned by the Asahi Shimbun are inherent to the
modern Japanese political structure. Explicitly outlined in the
Constitution of Japan (and discussed in greater detail in Chapter IV),
Article 9 serves as a direct legal barrier to Japanese proliferation.
Additionally, further symbolic statements by influential political
figures, such as former PM Eisaku Sato's Three Non-Nuclear Principles
and reiterations by former PMs Naoto Kan and Yoshihiko Noda have assured
both domestic and foreign entities that proliferation countervails the
very fabric of the modern Japanese society (Ministry of Foreign Affairs
of Japan, n.d. (B)). In the event of Japanese proliferation[, [Japan
would lose]{.mark} any semblance of [legitimacy]{.mark} as a benchmark
for the non-proliferation movement, and the durable restraint of Japan
as a motivator for other countries' non-proliferation regimes would
disappear.]{.underline} When a role model goes against the very idea
that it supported and valued, it will both lose its adherents and
disenfranchise the ideology itself. Japan is an exemplar of advocating
for and upholding non-proliferation, and if it is not possible for Japan
to remain in said position, it is unlikely that another, less qualified
country will thereafter be able to inherit it. The environment that this
would produce is that of ideological anarchy surrounding
non-proliferation. With their benchmark defunct, countries would no
longer feel obligated, even on an informal basis, to practice restraint
with regard to the production of nuclear weapons.8 The East Asian
[Disequilibrium With the NPT rapidly disintegrating and the ideology of
non-proliferation in a state of disrepair, [Japan's]{.mark} immediate
[neighbors would]{.mark} be the first to [suffer]{.mark} from its
proliferation]{.underline}. As it stands[, [Japan, South Korea]{.mark}
(ROK), and [Taiwan]{.mark} (ROC) do not possess nuclear weapons despite
their neighbors, North Korea (DPRK) and China (PRC), possessing
them]{.underline}. Rather, [the [nuclear umbrella]{.mark} under which
Japan and the ROK exist **[provide]{.mark}s [a counterbalance]{.mark}**
[to]{.mark} the **nuclear [capabilities]{.mark}** [of]{.mark} the
**[DPRK]{.mark}** and the **PRC**.9 Japan's acquisition of nuclear
weapons would entirely confound this delicate balance]{.underline}. Onx
the side of Japan's allies[, the ROK and ROC may perceive a nuclearized
Japan as a threat to their own sovereignties both directly and through
the threat of nuclear escalation with their neighbors (the DPRK and
PRC), and they may attempt to match Japan's proliferation with their own
campaigns of nuclear procurement]{.underline}. Presently, the President
of the Republic of Korea, Yoon Suk-yeol has already expressed his
willingness to begin the development of nuclear weapons, and --- due to
this existing willingness to proliferate --- [[Japan's
prolif]{.mark}eration [would]{.mark} likely [serve as the]{.mark}
[**catalyst** for the ROK's]{.mark} proprietary [development of
nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}]{.underline} 50 and Sukin 2023).
Regarding the ROC, the situation would be less dire on account of
President Tsai Ing-wen's staunchly anti-nuclear views; nevertheless, as
non-proliferation loses its legitimacy and the ROC gains exposure to new
regional threats, this attitude would likely change (AP News 2019). [For
Japan's nuclear neighbors, with whom relations exist in a precarious
state, its nuclear proliferation may give reason for the procurement of
increasingly powerful nuclear arsenals in an attempt to preempt a
potential strike from Japan and 'rebalance' the nuclear
ecosystem]{.underline}. With both effects in mind, [[an **arms race**
among a nuclearized East Asia would occur]{.underline}]{.mark} at a
minimum, and --- in the worst case --- [the nuclear arms race
originating in East Asia would **spread sequentially** from country to
country]{.underline} in a centrifugal manner.

### Russia Alignment

#### Current lack of US alignment with Japan [forces]{.underline} cooperation with Russia- energy policy, shipping routes

**Almazova-Ilyana '20** \[July 2020. AB Almazova-Ilyana. IOP conference
series: Earth and environmental science. Vol. 539. No. 1. IOP

Now let us analyze aforementioned findings to highlight Japan's national
interests that are confronted by existing challenges and pursued in its
policies in respect to its involvement in the Arctic. Firstly, among
Japan's interests in the region are those that fall under the term
'security' in its broader sense. There are not many concerns about
traditional military security since all major stakeholders agree that
the Arctic should stay a region of cooperation and retain its peaceful
character \[13\]. Tonami Aki notes that Japanese research conducted
under auspices of the Ministry of Defense 'concluded that the changes in
the Arctic do not pose direct security threats to Japan' \[14\].
However, there are concerns about physical security in regards to
natural disasters facilitated by rapid climate change in the Arctic. To
address these worries Japan will need to continue extensive research on
possible threats as well as develop reliable response mechanisms.
[Another [Japanese interest]{.mark} in question is [linked to energy
security.]{.mark} A country that [imports 90% of its energy]{.mark}
resources crucially [needs]{.mark} a [diversification]{.mark} of those
both in terms of type and supplier.]{.underline} Currently Japan is

**relations with Russia**]{.mark} since it **[does not follow US and EU

crisis.]{.underline} [[Cooperation with Russia on Arctic mineral
resources development seems to be an **important step** towards Japan's
energy security]{.underline}]{.mark}. Final notion in the "security"
section is the commonly overlooked food security. Certain marine
species' northward migration and threats associated with it that we
mentioned above must be addressed to provide the nation with a healthy
and rich diet. For an island state with limited land space it is a vital
issue. Again this area heavily relies on research and risks assessment.
Secondly, [there are [obvious **economic interests**]{.mark} to be met
by Arctic exploration]{.underline}. One aspect of it is energy resources
development discussed above. From the economic point of view there are
still debates over its cost-effectiveness, so Japan is quite prudent.
The Arctic Routes started playing a significant role in Japan\'s
shipping. The most significant one was the Northern Sea Route, which was
connecting Japan and Europe. It was used for more frequent trade flows,
as the Route was 40% shorter than the previously used one through the
Suez Canal. In the light of the Economic Partnership Agreement between
Japan and the EU that entered into force last year Japan is now
particularly interested in the NSR shipping as it would benefit Japan-EU
trade and help significantly increase trade volumes. [Japan is also
contemplating the possibility of becoming an Asian maritime hub in the
beginning of the Arctic Sea Route]{.underline}. Authorities of Hokkaido
consider the sea port of Tomakomai as the potential center for Arctic

**relies heavily** on Russian authorities and their commitment to make

[Russian government's initiatives like 'Arctic 2035' strategy are
promising to bring drastic infrastructure development and quality of

shipbuilding, engineering and facility construction, but here Japan
meets fierce competition from Korean and especially Chinese companies.
Thirdly, [[Japan]{.mark} [seeks stronger position and influence in
international politics]{.mark}]{.underline}. [It wants to [**actively

them]{.mark}]{.underline} -- [a right that is currently almost
exclusively enjoyed by the Arctic states.]{.underline} Considering
impact that developments in the region have on the rest of the world
this wish seems legitimate. Other dimension of this interest is
competition between CJK countries. At the current point in time when the
whole world is watching China continue its rise, Japan will certainly
not put up with being just "China's neighbor". High- aiming ambitions of
South Korea also do not leave Japan a chance to just "go with the flow".
CJK countries keep constantly pushing each other forward, and despite
all the rivalry it is more of an advantage for them. In regards to other
East Asian states Japan has to promote positive global change, including
dealing with Arctic challenges facilitated by climate change, to improve
its image within the region. Overcoming militaristic perceptions of the
past by doing good perfectly fits into the framework of Japanese public
diplomacy to Southeast Asia. Last, but not least, [Japan is deeply
invested in tackling environmental problems in the Arctic. These issues
are not only contextualized as security threats, but also comply with
greater consensus on the need for urgent environmental action to protect
the planet.]{.underline} Japan shows great commitment to the United
Nations Sustainable Development Goals and principles of the Paris
Agreement. Japan utilizes its advanced research and observation carried

### US Alliance Scenario

#### Japanese consultation over the [plan]{.underline} is key to [broader]{.underline} reinvigoration of the alliance

**Tatsumi '25** (4/4/2025, Yuki, "Back to the Future? The Implications
of Growing Strategic Competition in the Arctic for the US-Japan
Alliance,"
https://www.stimson.org/2025/implications-strategic-competition-arctic-us-japan-alliance/)

Fast forward to the 21st century, and [[Japan]{.mark} has [not]{.mark}
been [oblivious to]{.mark} the rising [importance of]{.mark} the
[Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}, either. The Japanese government, following
Japan's ascendance to the Arctic Council as a permanent observer in
2013, issued its Arctic Policy in 2015 which focuses on (1) environment,
(2) indigenous population, (3) science and technology, (4) promoting the
rule of law, (5) utilization of the Arctic passage route, (6) natural
resource development, and (7) regional security. This policy was
incorporated into Japan's Third Basic Plan for Ocean Policy, most
recently updated in 2018. Despite the regional security consideration,
Japan's approach to the Arctic has been firmly rooted in three areas --
scientific research, promotion of international cooperation, and
sustainable development of the Arctic. However, [as the [geostrategic
competition]{.mark} in the Arctic seems to [intensify]{.mark}, the
[North Pacific]{.mark} may [re-enter]{.mark} [Japan's strategic

[[China]{.underline}]{.mark} on engaging the Arctic countries through
investments in infrastructure and energy developments in the region [has
[compelled Japan to work with U.S.]{.mark} and European partners to
ensure that the Arctic remains "free and open and is governed by the
rule of law".]{.underline} Indeed, Japan has been reenergizing its
outreach to Northern Europe -- When then-foreign minister Yoko Kamikawa
visited Sweden in January 2024, she unveiled Japan's Northern European
Diplomacy Initiative with four focus areas: the Arctic, gender issues,
broader economic cooperation (including cooperation in science and
technology), and defense. While cooperation in the Arctic under this
initiative is centered in cooperation in scientific research,
environmental preservation, and human resources development, the
countries that have high stakes in the Arcti,c such as Sweden, Norway,
Denmark, and Finland have been identified as the countries with which
Japan seeks to strengthen its defense relations. What's more, China's
growing cooperation with Russia only aggravates Japan's concern of
China's behavior in the Arctic. North Pacific in the U.S.-Japan Alliance
Given the emergence of the Arctic as the next ground for geostrategic
competition, security [[cooperation]{.mark} [in]{.mark} the
[Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline} and the broader North Pacific [[may
re-emerge]{.mark} as places for [deeper U.S.-Japan
alliance]{.mark}]{.underline} cooperation as Japan looks to push for
greater cooperation with Northern European countries. [In fact, despite
rising tensions in the trans-Atlantic alliance over the handling of the
Russia-Ukraine war and trade, the [Arctic provides a unique opportunity
for greater cooperation]{.mark}]{.underline}. Indeed, [[Japan's
approach]{.mark} to the Arctic---primarily [focused on scientific
research]{.mark} and environmental conservation---makes Tokyo
well-positioned to facilitate]{.underline} the cooperation between the
U.S., Japan, and North Europe at a critical juncture for the future of
the trans-Atlantic alliance. In the context of the U.S.-Japan alliance,
Japan can leverage the assets and capabilities that the Japan Maritime
Self-Defense Force (JMSDF) has already developed to enhance its role in
surveillance and intelligence-gathering in the North Pacific. Even after
the Cold War, the JMSDF maintained its surveillance and
intelligence-gathering operations in the North to keep not only North
