### Solvency\-\--Say Yes\-\--2NC

#### Japan wants cooperation

**Tatsumi 25** \[Yuki Tatsumi is a Senior Fellow and Co-Director of the
East Asia Program and Director of the Japan Program at the Stimson
Center (4-4-2025; Back to the Future? The Implications of Growing
Strategic Competition in the Arctic for the US-Japan Alliance • Stimson
Center; 7-6-2025; Stimson Center;
https://www.stimson.org/2025/implications-strategic-competition-arctic-us-japan-alliance/\]

[[Given the emergence of the Arctic as the next ground for geostrategic
competition]{.mark}, security cooperation in [the Arctic]{.mark} and the
broader North Pacific [may re-emerge as places for deeper U.S.-Japan
alliance]{.mark}]{.underline} cooperation as Japan looks to push for
greater cooperation with Northern European countries. In fact, [despite
rising tensions in the trans-Atlantic alliance over the handling of the
Russia-Ukraine war and trade, the Arctic provides a unique opportunity
for greater cooperation.]{.underline} Indeed[, [Japan's approach to the
Arctic]{.mark}---primarily [focused on scientific research and]{.mark}
environmental [conservation]{.mark}---[makes]{.mark} [Tokyo
well-positioned to facilitate the cooperation between the U.S., Japan,
and North Europe]{.mark}]{.underline} at a critical juncture for the
future of the trans-Atlantic alliance. [In the context of the
U.S.-Japan]{.underline} alliance, [Japan can leverage the assets and
capabilities that the Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force (JMSDF) has
already developed to enhance its role in surveillance and
intelligence-gathering in the North Pacific]{.underline}. Even after the
Cold War, the JMSDF maintained its surveillance and
intelligence-gathering operations in the North to keep not only North
Korea's but also China's and Russia's military behavior in check. These
efforts may need to be enhanced once again, especially given the
elevated level of military activities by China and Russia. The biggest
challenge for Japan may lie in resource allocation. Japan has spent the
last 10-15 years redirecting its defense investment and its force
posture to focus more on defending the Southwestern Islands. The
resurgence of the Cold War-era challenge from the North could complicate
the ongoing effort, as Japan now has to respond to the security concern
in the North once again. However, as Japan anticipates a U.S. request to
"do more" in the U.S.-Japan alliance, enhancing its investment to
support Japan's capability for North Pacific security can offer a
meaningful option.

#### Japan wants to cooperate with the US

**Hayata 24** \[Sakikio Hayata Research Fellow at the Ocean Policy
Research Institute of the Sasakawa Peace Foundation in Japan ("Japan\'s
Arctic Policy: Current Status and Challenges", Asian Pacific Bulietin,
8/15/24
https://scholarspace.manoa.hawaii.edu/server/api/core/bitstreams/1e59a719-5e4e-46b6-bb92-bc5e4d1d59f9/content\]

[[Science and science diplomacy have always been key priorities for
Japan]{.underline}.]{.mark} During the negotiations of the 2017
Agreement on Scientific Cooperation under the auspices of the Arctic
Council, [Japan was one of the few key observer states that positively
contributed to the negotiations]{.underline}. [[Japan has been actively
involved in Arctic research]{.mark} [through various]{.mark} projects
and [collaborations]{.mark}]{.underline}. For example, the Arctic
Challenge for Sustainability (ArCS) project launched in 2015 aims to
enhance Japan\'s Arctic research capabilities and promote international
cooperation. This project elucidates changes in the climate and
environment and clarifies their effects on human society. The ArCS
project also provides accurate projections and environmental assessments
to internal and external stakeholders, enabling them to make appropriate
decisions regarding the sustainable development of the Arctic region.
[[Japan is also investing in]{.mark} advanced o[bservation
tech]{.mark}nologies such as satellite remote sensing and autonomous
underwater vehicles [to monitor and study the Arctic
environment]{.mark}]{.underline}. These technologies provide critical
data for understanding the impact of climate change and supporting
evidence-based policymaking. Having briefly reviewed the history and
content of Japan\'s Arctic policy, this article now touches upon the
impact of Russia\'s invasion of Ukraine. The conflict has had
significant repercussions for Arctic cooperation. In response, the
Arctic States, excluding Russia, issued a joint statement condemning the
invasion and suspended their participation in all Arctic Council
meetings. This suspension halted cooperation and dialogue in all Arctic
areas, including scientific research. While the Arctic Council\'s
Working Groups and Expert Groups have resumed their activities in a
limited fashion, prospects remain uncertain. In April 2023, the Cabinet
approved the Fourth Basic Plan on Ocean Policy, which continues to
position Arctic policy as a \"key measure to be steadily promoted\" in
the Plan. The Plan also addresses the impact of the invasion of Ukraine
as outlined below: Currently, due to the impact of Russia\'s invasion of
Ukraine, some Arctic-related activities, including the Arctic Council,
have been suspended, creating uncertainty about the future of the Arctic
situation. However, our country remains committed to advancing the three
pillars of its Arctic policy---research and development, international
cooperation, and sustainable use. We will continue to exchange
information with relevant countries and ensure thorough preparation for
all possible scenarios (author's translation). Since their resumption in
2024, Japan has dispatched experts and government officials to Arctic
Council working groups and task forces, thereby enhancing its
contributions to Arctic science. However, given the Council's limited
functionality, the efficacy of Japan's contributions is questionable.
[[Despite the complications]{.mark} and tensions surrounding Arctic
cooperation at the moment]{.underline}, [[Japan is committed to
remaining an active and increasingly more prominent advocate for
scientific endeavor]{.mark}, [multinational collaboration, and
sustainability in Arctic affairs]{.mark}]{.underline}. This commitment
is encapsulated in the Fourth Basic Plan. In accordance with the Plan,
[Japan will "always consider the optimal combination of bilateral and
multilateral approaches, further promote exchanges of views with Arctic
and other relevant countries, maximize the use of international
frameworks related to the Arctic, strengthen the dissemination of our
country\'s perspectives and achievements in observation and
research]{.underline}, and enhance our presence (author's translation)."

#### New Japanese Artic Policy prove say yes

**Pham 24** \[Khang Pham is an Author at the Artic Institute (4-16-2024;
Japan Steps Up Its Arctic Engagement; 7-6-2025; Arctic Institute -
Center for Circumpolar Security Studies;
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/japan-steps-up-arctic-engagement\]

Adopted in October 2015, [Japan's Arctic
Policy](https://www8.cao.go.jp/ocean/english/arctic/pdf/japans_ap_e.pdf)
covered a wide range of topics. These included global environmental
issues, indigenous peoples of the Arctic, science and technology, the
rule of law, the promotion of international cooperation, the Arctic Sea
Route, natural resources development, and national security. However,
for a more comprehensive understanding of Japan's engagement with the
Arctic, one should look at Japan's Basic Plan on Ocean Policy. The Plan
provides a clearer picture of the evolving thought process behind
Japanese policymaking related to the Arctic. The Basic Plan on Ocean
Policy is reviewed and updated every five years to ensure its relevance
and effectiveness in addressing the evolving maritime issues. The Arctic
first appeared in the [Second Basic Plan on Ocean
Policy](https://www8.cao.go.jp/ocean/english/plan/pdf/plan02_e.pdf) in
2013. This plan outlined three directions for the Arctic: observing and
studying the region from a global perspective, increasing international
cooperation, and utilizing the Arctic Sea Route. On April 28, 2023,
Japan released its [fourth
version](https://www8.cao.go.jp/ocean/policies/plan/plan04/pdf/keikaku_honbun.pdf)
of the Basic Plan on Ocean Policy, which further demonstrated the
country's growing engagement in the Polar North. While the fundamental
priorities around research, international cooperation, and sustainable
utilization remain consistent across the Second and [Third Basic Plan on
Ocean
Policy](https://www8.cao.go.jp/ocean/policies/plan/plan04/pdf/keikaku_honbun.pdf),
the updated policy reveals meaningful evolutions that showcase Japan
assuming a more assertive Arctic identity to match its interests with
influence. The latest document is currently not available in English,
and this op-ed provides a summary of its main Arctic-related content.
**What's new in Japan's Arctic Engagement?** [[The Fourth Basic Plan on
Ocean Policy reaffirms Japan's commitment to]{.mark} (1) [enhancing
Arctic research capabilities]{.mark} to guide its Arctic-related
decisions, (2) [fostering international scientific cooperation]{.mark},
and (3) nurturing the next generation of talent to address Arctic
issues]{.underline}. It underscores the importance of contributing to
international forums such as the Arctic Council, and cooperative
frameworks with other Arctic states, to help shape rules and norms. The
plan also emphasizes the importance of creating a safe environment for
Japanese shipping companies when utilizing the Arctic Sea Route by using
satellite data to create sea ice charts, thereby enhancing Arctic
navigation safety. It also encourages the Japanese business community's
active participation in international forums such as the Arctic Economic
Council and the Arctic Circle to expand Arctic region economic
activities, while prioritizing sustainable Arctic resource development
and shipping route use. What are the new elements in this updated
version? **Research** The latest plan provides more concrete details on
developing technologies to strengthen Arctic observation capabilities,
including constructing a new ice-breaking research vessel. Scheduled for
completion in 2026, [the
vessel](https://www.jamstec.go.jp/parv/e/overview/#spec) promises
unmatched observational capabilities and icebreaking capacity to access
remote sections of the Arctic Ocean. Collaborative projects such as [the
Arctic Challenge for Sustainability Initiative II (ArCS
II)](https://www.nipr.ac.jp/arcs2/e/) are set to not only accelerate
discovery and ensure these insights actively shape Japan's Arctic agenda
in real-time, but also develop human resources who can lead
international discussions to solve problems in the Arctic.
**International cooperation** The fourth version puts more emphasis on
adhering to international law principles such as freedom of navigation
in the Arctic Ocean, which is aligned with its [Free and Open
Indo-Pacific
Vision](https://www.mofa.go.jp/policy/other/bluebook/2019/html/chapter1/c0102.html#sf01).
The updated document also reflects recent negotiations culminating in a
2021 legally binding
[agreement](https://www.arcticwwf.org/newsroom/features/the-central-arctic-ocean-fisheries-agreement-what-it-is-is-not-and-might-be/).
The agreement, which came into force in June 2021, bans unregulated
[fishing](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/climate-change-factor-impacting-current-future-commercial-fisheries-arctic-region/)
in Central Arctic Ocean international waters. As an active participant
in these talks since 2015, Japan strictly adheres to this treaty on
sustainable fisheries management regimen. This approach balances
imminent economic opportunity with long-term conservation needs in the
face of warming, acidic waters, and shifting fish stocks. **Sustainable
utilization** The area of sustainable utilization is particularly
exciting as it features the most updated elements in the plan's fourth
version. While it does not address specific natural, technical,
institutional, and economic issues related to the route as its
predecessors, it provides more detailed strategies to support Japanese
shipping companies in utilizing the Arctic Sea Route[. [This
includes]{.mark} (1) collecting information on the Arctic region and the
Arctic Sea Route, and (2) [fostering cooperation between industry,
academia, and government, with an emphasis on information sharing. The
plan also prioritizes climate change mitigation]{.mark} and Arctic
environmental conservation]{.underline}, which is in line with the
"[Long-Term Strategy for Growth Based on the Paris
Agreement](https://unfccc.int/sites/default/files/resource/Japan_LTS2021.pdf)"
and the "[Plan for Global Warming
Countermeasures](https://www.env.go.jp/content/900505720.pdf)."
**Conclusion** [[The Fourth Basic Plan on Ocean Policy]{.mark} [reveals
a]{.mark} notably [more proactive]{.mark}, assertive, and ambitious
[tone]{.mark} [across]{.mark} the main pillars of [research,
international cooperation]{.mark}, and sustainable utilization than its
predecessors.]{.underline} In particular, with the fourth version
further empowering sustainable utilization, Japan cements itself as an
engaged steward rather than a bystander across Arctic development to set
the standard for the entire region. This nuance shows Japan's pragmatic
understanding of the complex dynamics shaping the 21^st^-century Arctic
amid climate change and internationalization. In an age of climate
crisis and growing [great power
competition](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/return-great-power-competition-arctic/),
Japan faces increasing incentives to engage itself across the Arctic
region's research, governance, and emerging commerce landscape. As
demonstrated in the fourth version, the country asserts itself as an
essential partner in stewarding and studying this vital region alongside
fellow concerned nations. Both international collaboration and domestic
coordination are key vehicles for Japan to match its ambitions with its
capabilities in an increasingly busy Polar North.

### Say No\-\--2AC

#### Japan doesn't want to militarize the artic 

**Dervovic et al 25** \[Mendy Dervovic PhD Candidate, Department of Law,
Reykjavík University and a visiting researcher at the University of
Lapland Artic Center, Dr Osamu Inagaki is a researcher at the Polar

Suggestions for the Next Decade*. Polar Cooperation Research Centre
(PCRC), Kobe University. ArCS II International Law Briefing Paper Series
No. 12 https://doi.org/10.24546/0100494092\]

However, a separate investigation is required to determine how this
change in the balance of power is specifically related to 'security
developments in the Arctic', which [[Japan's Arctic Policy
identifies]{.underline}]{.mark} as requiring careful attention, and the
extent to which this change has influenced the Policy's implementation.
The [2015 Arctic Policy]{.underline} recognises that the opening of the
Arctic sea route and the availability of new resources will serve as new
sources of friction among countries. It is also acknowledged that as a
result, ['\[[s\]ome Arctic states]{.mark}, with a view toward securing
their national interests and protecting their territories, [have become
active in]{.mark} the area of [national defense]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Moves toward expanding military presence may have an impact on the
international security environment'. Therefore, [[for Japan, 'it is
important to prevent moves to strengthen military]{.mark} [presence in
the region]{.mark}]{.underline} from leading to tension and
confrontations'. It recognises that moves to [[enhance military]{.mark}
presence [could alter the security]{.mark} environment [not only in the
Arctic but also around Japan]{.mark}]{.underline}. Accordingly, it
states that [Japan must 'pay close attention to moves by the states
concerned]{.underline} and also to promote cooperation with the Arctic
and other states'. Following the announcement of the 2018 Basic Plan,
[[Minister of Foreign Affairs]{.mark} Kono [stated]{.mark} in his
keynote speech at the Arctic Circle, that ['\[n\]o one wants]{.mark} the
[Arctic]{.mark} to be a place where interests collide and
[conflicts]{.mark} are solved by power']{.underline} (2018 Foreign
Minister Kono Speech, 4). The 2023 Basic Plan states---although not
necessarily in a security-specific context---that the impact of Russia's
aggression of Ukraine has made the future circumstances surrounding the
Arctic uncertain. In response, the Plan states that Japan will maintain
the three pillars of its Arctic Policy while 'continuing to exchange
information with relevant states and making thorough preparations for
all scenarios'. The 2024 Priority Strategy adopts mostly identical
wording. In other words, [Japan's Arctic policy]{.underline} documents
have [undergone no significant changes]{.underline} in their wording or
in the key concerns [over the last decade]{.underline}.

### No Russian Alignment\-\--2AC

#### Japan won't cooperate with Russia

**Hong 25** - Dr. Nong Hong holds a PhD of interdisciplinary study of

Russia-Ukraine conflict]{.mark}]{.underline}. [While [Japanese
firms]{.mark} still hold stakes in legacy projects like
[Sakhalin-2](https://www.reuters.com/business/energy/rival-lng-supplies-sakhalins-depleting-fields-give-japan-an-exit-russian-gas-2024-12-11/),
they [have frozen new Arctic investments and suspended
participation]{.mark} in ventures such as Arctic LNG 2 [due to sanctions

### No Nuclearization\-\--2AC

#### Japan is still committed to no nukes

**Kim and Lee, 2023** \[Tongfi - professor in Asian geopolitics at the
Centre for Security, Diplomacy and Strategy (CSDS) of the Brussels
School of Governance, and Do Young - postdoctoral fellow in the
Department of Political Science at the University of Oslo, "Continuity
and changes: the effects of Russia's war against Ukraine on Japanese and
South Korean nuclear-weapons discourse," Publication Cover The
Nonproliferation Review, 30: 4-6,
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/10736700.2024.2435143#abstract\]

[As Russia has made barely veiled nuclear threats against Ukraine and
NATO, a sense has emerged in East Asia that the role of nuclear weapons
may change]{.underline} globally.Footnote3 As other articles in this
special issue show, countries across the world are adapting to the new
security landscape, and nuclear weapons are regaining a prominent place
in it. East Asia is no exception to this sentiment. [[The war in Ukraine
has heightened Japan's]{.underline}]{.mark} and South Korea's [[security
concerns, sparking]{.mark} a reassessment of external nuclear threats
and [debate]{.mark}s [about]{.mark} various [nuclear]{.mark}-deterrent
[options]{.mark}]{.underline}. For Tokyo and Seoul, the new developments
in European security and nuclear-weapons discourse are highly relevant.
Like the European members of NATO, [[Japan]{.mark} and South Korea [rely
heavily on]{.mark} the [extended deterrence]{.mark} provided [by the
U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates. They have therefore closely watched
Washington's policy toward Ukraine and Russia.]{.underline} Although
Russia itself has presented limited military threats to the two East
Asian states since the end of the Cold War, they are concerned about
Moscow's blatant challenge to the territorial order of the international
system. After all, Japan and South Korea also face the threats of
aggressive and autocratic neighbors, China and North Korea, albeit to
different degrees.

[This article examines]{.underline} the continuity and changes in
Jap[anese and South Korean discourse on nuclear weapons before and after
the Russian invasion of Ukraine]{.underline}. The publics in the two
countries have exhibited markedly different attitudes toward nuclear
weapons since February 2022. [[While Japan has experienced]{.mark}
significant [shifts in]{.mark} its broad national-[security discourse,
its discourse on nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark} [has remained]{.mark}
largely [unchanged]{.mark}.]{.underline} In contrast, South Korea's
nuclear discourse has undergone substantial changes since the onset of
the war. Although norms concerning nuclear weapons have evidently
influenced both countries, strategic considerations regarding the
intensity of threats from their main external adversaries and the likely
types of external aggression have played significant roles in shaping
nuclear discourse.

More specifically, [**[Japan has maintained its distance from nuclear
options]{.underline}**, [focusing on]{.underline}]{.mark} [bolstering
its [conventional capabilities]{.mark} to counter the nonexistential
threat from China]{.underline} and the risk of aerial or naval
aggression. [In Japan]{.underline}, where [[the nuclear taboo]{.mark}
since 1945 [has been very strong, even the shock of the war has not been
enough to turn public opinion]{.mark}]{.underline}.Footnote4 Although
there were important changes in Japan's national-security discourse,
continuity prevailed in the nuclear-weapons discourse. Conversely, South
Korea has actively explored various nuclear-deterrent options to deter
the existential threat from North Korea and the potential risk of ground
attacks. In South Korea, where there was already significant support for
acquiring nuclear weapons prior to the onset of the Ukraine war, this
support has increased even further. According to a synthesis of four
different periodic public-opinion surveys released by four South Korean
research institutions, the average public support for the option of
independent nuclear armament was 66.1 percent in the first surveys
conducted after February 2022. This represents an increase of 4.7
percentage points from the average support found in the last surveys
conducted before February 2022.Footnote5 South Korea's government
discourse on nuclear-weapons policy, supported by public opinion and
further accelerated by the Yoon Suk Yeol administration's "peace through
strength" approach, has undergone significant changes since the
beginning of the war. As we will explain later, while it is tempting to
attribute the two countries' diverging trajectories solely to nuclear
norms and public opinion, the discourse of policy elites and strategic
calculations also need to be taken into account.Footnote6

#### Japan will not nuclearize -- official documents and public opinion 

**Kim and Lee, 2023** \[Tongfi - professor in Asian geopolitics at the
Centre for Security, Diplomacy and Strategy (CSDS) of the Brussels
School of Governance, and Do Young - postdoctoral fellow in the
Department of Political Science at the University of Oslo, "Continuity
and changes: the effects of Russia's war against Ukraine on Japanese and
South Korean nuclear-weapons discourse," Publication Cover The
Nonproliferation Review, 30: 4-6,
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/10736700.2024.2435143#abstract\]

[[On December]{.mark} 16, [2022, the Japanese government approved three
major documents on]{.mark} its security and [defense
policy]{.mark}]{.underline} at a cabinet meeting---the National Security
Strategy, the National Defense Strategy, and the Defense Buildup
Program. [[The]{.underline}]{.mark} three [[documents marked a]{.mark}n
important [shift]{.mark} in Japan's defense policy
[in]{.mark}]{.underline} areas such as [its pursuit of [conventional
counterstrike capabilities]{.mark} and]{.underline} a drastic increase
in [defense spending]{.underline} (from around 1 percent to 2 percent of
gross domestic product by fiscal year 2027). The National Defense
Strategy points out that an "unprecedented situation is unfolding," as
Russia "has launched an outright aggression against Ukraine, repeatedly
taken actions and sent messages that can be interpreted as threat
\[sic\] of nuclear weapons use."Footnote29

The three documents discuss threats of nuclear weapons many times,
[[but]{.underline} [the]{.underline}]{.mark} [National Security
[Strategy states]{.mark} that "[Japan will]{.mark} adhere to the basic
policy of [maintain]{.mark}ing [an exclusively]{.mark} national
[defense-oriented policy, not becoming a military power]{.mark} that
poses a threat to other countries, [and observing]{.mark} the Three
[Non-Nuclear Principles]{.mark}]{.underline} \[of non-possession,
non-production, and non-introduction of nuclear weapons\]."Footnote30
The three documents discuss nuclear weapons to protect Japan only in the
context of the US nuclear umbrella over Japan:

Japan will fundamentally reinforce its defense capabilities and deepen
security cooperation with the United States. By undertaking such
efforts, Japan will further strengthen the deterrence and response
capabilities of the Japan--U.S. Alliance, including extended deterrence
by the U.S. that is backed by its full range of capabilities, including
nuclear.Footnote31

Thus, the government [[discourse did not shift in]{.mark} a direction
that emphasizes the importance of nuclear weapons for Japan's national
security, let alone [support for]{.mark}]{.underline} nuclear sharing or
[[development]{.underline}]{.mark} [[of]{.underline}]{.mark} indigenous
[[nuc]{.underline}]{.mark}lear weapon[[s]{.underline}]{.mark}.

[Japan's [public opinion continued to demonstrate a strong anti-nuclear
norm]{.mark}]{.underline}, but there were also signs that Russia's
invasion of Ukraine was affecting the public's view of international
security. On the one hand, [80 percent]{.underline} of Japanese
respondents in 2023 [supported maintaining the Three Non-Nuclear
Principles, and [this makes it difficult for politicians to advocate a
change in]{.mark} Japan's [nuclear]{.mark}-weapons
[policy]{.mark}]{.underline}.Footnote32 On the other hand, the shock of
the Russian invasion and Moscow's threat of nuclear use also created a
rare political opportunity to talk about changing that policy. For
instance, former prime minister Shinzo Abe, on February 27, 2022,
remarked on television that discussion of nuclear sharing should not be
seen as taboo. His position was supported by Nippon Ishin, a
conservative opposition party.Footnote33 The public's reaction to Abe's
statement was initially difficult to interpret, as polls with different
wording showed widely varying levels of support for discussing nuclear
sharing, ranging from 20 percent to 57 percent.Footnote34 Although we
cannot be certain about the causal relationship, it is worth noting that
the percentage of the Japanese public who support Japan's participation
in the Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons decreased from 72
percent in 2020 and 71 percent in 2021 to 61 percent in 2022 and 2023
(surveyed in June and July each year).Footnote35

#### Evidence that says Japan will nuclearize is hedging, they won't actually do it

**Romei, 2023** \[Sayuri, "Watching Ukraine, South Korea and Japan eye
nuclear weapons. Here's what the US should do," 20 July 2023,
https://thebulletin.org/2023/07/watching-ukraine-south-korea-and-japan-eye-nuclear-weapons-heres-what-the-us-should-do/\]

Japan's nuclear hedging. [The [arguments against]{.mark} Japan's
[nuclearization]{.mark} traditionally [include]{.mark} domestic [public
opinion]{.mark} and the country's post-World War II [pacifist
identity]{.mark}]{.underline}, with others also citing [[technical and
financial hurdles, as well as]{.mark} the [enormous diplomatic
costs]{.mark}]{.underline} that such an endeavor would have. While most
of [these arguments still ring true]{.underline}, Japan's nuclear
hedging posture has played an important role against nuclearization.

Conservative Japanese politicians have a history of mixed messages
regarding the indigenous nuclear option. These messages are intended for
different and overlapping audiences. [[Statements]{.mark} [about
the]{.mark} constitutional [right to]{.mark} [possess nu]{.mark}clear
weapon[s]{.mark}]{.underline} exemplify such rhetoric and
[[are]{.underline}]{.mark} partly [[aimed at keeping Japan's]{.mark}

their]{.mark} neighbor's ultimate security
[intentions]{.mark}]{.underline}. At home, Japan is commonly viewed as a

to its highly advanced nuclear fuel cycle technologies.

[Japanese [officials]{.mark}]{.underline} have
[[use]{.underline}]{.mark}d [[the]{.mark} country's [refusal to develop
nu]{.mark}]{.underline}clear weapon[[s]{.mark}]{.underline} despite its
technological capabilities [[in two]{.underline}]{.mark} primary [[ways:
to reassure the public]{.mark} about the security of the nation, [while
maintaining its moral stance]{.mark}]{.underline} vis-à-vis global
peace. On some occasions, Japanese officials' allusions to a nuclear
option were directed to the United States and meant to test its
commitment to defend Japan. And pro-nuclear messages have also been
directed to the most conservative part of the Japanese public who find
inherent value in nuclear weapons, linking them to prestige and leverage
in international politics.

Although present throughout the postwar era, this ambiguous posture
flourished during the early years of Shinzo Abe's second tenure as prime
minister from 2012 to 2020. His decisively conservative figure sharply
contrasts with current Prime Minister Kishida's core values, a contrast
that also explains the recent containment of nuclear rhetoric in Japan.

Likewise, the resurgence of the nuclear option in South Korean security
discourse also caters to adversaries, the United States, and the most
conservative part of the domestic public. President Yoon acknowledged
the importance of strengthening his country's alliance with the United
States in the same breath as mentioning South Korea's possible
nuclearization. In short, he is using the playbook of past conservative
Japanese leaders.

[[Japan's nuclear hedging posture]{.underline}]{.mark}, which the
government uses to tailor its messaging to the different audiences, [[is
likely to remain]{.mark} in place. [It is hard to imagine Japan risking
this]{.mark} perfectly ambivalent [stance by seriously considering the
nuclear option]{.mark}]{.underline}---at least in the foreseeable
future.

#### Japan isn't worried about US deterrence

**Romei, 2023** \[Sayuri, "Watching Ukraine, South Korea and Japan eye

https://thebulletin.org/2023/07/watching-ukraine-south-korea-and-japan-eye-nuclear-weapons-heres-what-the-us-should-do/\]

Strengthening extended deterrence in the Indo-Pacific. [[Contrary to
what some assert, Tokyo]{.mark} and Seoul [do not seem to have]{.mark}
growing suspicions or [concerns vis-à-vis US]{.mark} extended
[deterrence]{.mark}.]{.underline} Certainly, both allies fear that, in
the future, Americans might elect another US administration---like that
of former President Donald Trump---that would undermine alliances. But,
despite the surge in nuclear rhetoric in Seoul, South Korea knows that
boosting its alliance with the United States remains its best option.
[[The]{.mark} Japanese [government is]{.mark} also [more determined than
ever to strengthen]{.mark} extended deterrence mechanisms and [its
alliance with the U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates]{.underline}.

and defense ministers]{.mark} both met with their US counterparts and
[confirmed an "unprecedented alignment]{.mark} of their vision,

upgrading nuclear dialogue to the ministerial level.

The timing is also right for a regional upgrade of extended deterrence
discussions. In March, Washington approached both Tokyo and Seoul with
the idea of establishing a trilateral consultative body on nuclear
deterrence. The two allies seemed to tentatively welcome the proposal.
But, given their contrasting threat assessments and deterrence fluency,
one can expect there will be some reluctance in both governments and
challenges that the three countries will have to overcome. Regular
trilateral tabletop exercises will also be needed, with scenarios that
continue playing until after a nuclear attack is launched.

### Latent Deterrence Solves\-\--2AC

#### Japan doesn't need to actually develop nukes -- the threat alone is enough

**Fuhrmann, 2025** \[Matthew - Professor of Political Science at Texas
A&M University, "U.S. Allies and Adversaries Are Attempting Nuclear
Deterrence without Weapons --- Will It Work?" 7 April 2025,
https://warontherocks.com/2025/04/u-s-allies-and-adversaries-are-attempting-nuclear-deterrence-\]

[U.S. allies in East Asia are relatively well positioned for success.
[Japan meets both criteria: it has a large-scale fissile
material]{.mark} production [capability and a restrained nuclear
program. **Nobody thinks that Japan is racing to a bomb**]{.mark}
**today. [But everybody knows]{.mark} that [it
could]{.mark}**]{.underline} build one quickly following a political
decision, especially if it just wanted a single first-generation weapon.
The key question is whether the Trump administration would oppose or
welcome a Japanese bomb. President Donald Trump has endorsed Japan
getting its own nuclear weapons in the past. If this becomes U.S.
policy, deterrence by proliferation would completely lose its bite.

However, the United States is not the only relevant audience for Japan's
latent deterrent. [[Tokyo]{.mark} has]{.underline} tried to [[use its
nuclear latency to deter China and North Korea]{.underline}]{.mark}, in
addition to influencing the United States. Then-vice president Joe
[[Biden did]{.mark} this on Tokyo's behalf [too,
telling]{.mark}]{.underline} Chinese leader [[Xi]{.underline}]{.mark}
Jinping that [[Japan could build nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}
"[virtually overnight]{.mark}]{.underline}" if Beijing didn't help rein
in North Korea. [[Japanese officials]{.mark} have]{.underline}
increasingly [[embrace]{.mark}d]{.underline} the idea of [[latent
deterrence]{.mark} against adversaries.]{.underline} Former Defense
Minister Satoshi Morimoto said, for example, [that [Japan's nuclear
latency provides "very great defensive]{.mark} deterrent
[functions]{.mark}." Japan's prospects for success here are
strong]{.underline} --- maybe better than towards the United States, due
to questions about Washington's opposition to nuclear proliferation.

South Korea is in a less favorable position. Yet, it is one in which
latent deterrence could still be viable. Like Japan, South Korea has
been able to credibly signal nuclear restraint in recent decades. But
unlike [Japan]{.underline}, it does not [have large-scale enrichment or
reprocessing facilities]{.underline}. Seoul's activities in this area
have been limited to laboratory-scale work, and it does not presently
have the technology needed to make enough fissile material for one or
more bombs. Based on my analysis, laboratory-scale enrichment or
reprocessing activities can be helpful for bolstering a latent
deterrent, but they are not nearly as effective as having larger-scale
plants.

### Black Carbon Defense\-\--2AC

#### [Ships]{.underline} and [Russia]{.underline} makes black carbon is [inevitable]{.underline} or the [IMO solves]{.underline}.

Karen **McVeigh 22**. senior news reporter for the Guardian since
December 2006. "'Black carbon' threat to Arctic as sea routes open up
with global heating." Guardian. 4-1-2022.
https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2022/apr/10/black-carbon-threat-to-arctic-as-sea-routes-open-up-with-global-heating

In February last year, [a **[Russian gas tanker]{.mark}**]{.underline},
Christophe de Margerie, [[made **history** by **navigating**]{.mark}
**the icy [waters]{.mark}** [of the]{.mark}]{.underline} [[northern
**sea route**]{.mark} **in mid-winter**]{.underline}. The [**pioneering
voyage**,]{.underline} from Jiangsu in China to a remote Arctic port in
Siberia, [was **heralded** as the [start of a **new era**]{.mark} that
could **reshape**]{.underline} global **[shipping routes]{.underline}**
-- cutting travel times between Europe and Asia by more than a third.

It has been made possible by the climate crisis. Shrinking polar ice has
allowed shipping traffic in the Arctic to rise 25% between 2013 and 2019
and the growth is expected to continue.

But Arctic shipping is not only made possible by the climate crisis, it
is adding to it too. [[More ships mean a **rise in**]{.mark} **exhaust
[fumes]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [which is [accelerating]{.mark} **ice
melt** in this **sensitive region**]{.underline} due to a complex
phenomenon [involving "**[black carbon]{.mark}**]{.underline}", an air
pollutant formed by the incomplete combustion of fossil fuels.

When black carbon, or soot, lands on snow and ice, it dramatically
speeds up melting. Dark snow and ice, by absorbing more energy, melts
far faster than heat-reflecting white snow, creating a vicious circle of
faster warming.

Environmentalists warn that the Arctic, which is warming four times
faster than the global average, has seen an 85% rise in black carbon
from ships between 2015 and 2019, mainly because of the increase in oil
tankers and bulk carriers.

The particles, which exacerbate respiratory and cardiovascular illness
in towns, are short-term but potent climate agents: they represent more
than 20% of carbon dioxide equivalent emissions from ships, according to
one estimate.

We're hitting this cascading tipping point for the climate ... we need
to do something about black carbon urgently

[Yet unlike other **transport sectors**]{.underline}, including road,
rail and inland waterways, [where **air-quality standards curb
emissions**]{.underline}, [no **regulations exist for
shipping**]{.underline}. Last November, [the]{.underline} International
Maritime Organization ([[IMO]{.underline}]{.mark}) [[adopted a
resolution on the use of]{.mark} [cleaner fuels in the **Arctic** to
**reduce black carbon**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[but left it as a
voluntary move]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Last week, [the [IMO]{.mark} was once again in the
**spotlight**]{.underline}. A [[coalition]{.mark} of **environmental
groups** [warned a meeting of its]{.mark}
[pollution]{.mark}]{.underline}, prevention [[and]{.mark} **response
[subcommittee]{.mark}** [that its **resolution** did **too
little**]{.mark} **to tackle the Arctic's**]{.underline} climate
**[crisis]{.underline}**. They submitted a paper calling on governments
to agree mandatory regulations to slash shipping's emissions of black
carbon in the region.

"We're hitting this cascading tipping point for the climate," said Dr
Lucy Gilliam, senior shipping policy officer of Seas at Risk. "With the
IPCC report, we are seeing again why we need to do something about black
carbon urgently."

Last Monday, scientists from the UN's Intergovernmental Panel on Climate
Change (IPCC) warned it was "now or never" for action to stave off
climate breakdown. They concluded that the international community was
underperforming on climate commitments, but singled out the shipping
sector and the IMO for particular criticism.

The pollution from global shipping increased by 4.9% in 2021, according
to a report by the shipbrokers Simpson Spence Young.

"IMO member states must agree on ambitious and urgent global action to
dramatically reduce ship-source black carbon emissions this decade, in
order to mitigate the climate crisis in the Arctic," said Dr Sian Prior,
lead adviser to the Clean Arctic Alliance, a coalition of 21 non-profit
groups lobbying governments to protect Arctic wildlife and people. She
urged states and regions to do their part by acting immediately to cut
black carbon from ships.

If all shipping using heavy fuel oil in the Arctic switched to cleaner
distillate fuel, it would cut their black carbon emissions by 44%, the
Alliance said. Heavy fuel oil or bunker fuel is a viscous, low-grade,
cheap oil contaminated with substances including nitrogen and sulphur,
which make it more polluting than distillate.

If all ships also installed diesel particulate filters, which reduce
emissions by capturing and storing soot, black carbon could be cut by a
further 90%.

However, others argue that the IMO's 2021 ban on heavy fuel oils in the
Arctic -- a move aimed at reducing the risk of spillage and expected to
come into effect in 2029 -- will see a reduction in black carbon.

"The tide is swimming in the same direction already," said Paul
Blomerus, director of Clear Seas: Centre for Responsible Marine
Shipping, an independent research institute in Canada funded by industry
and government. "Many Canadian-flagged ships are moving towards
distillate fuels, ahead of the IMO ban, which will have the added effect
of reducing black carbon emissions.

"You could argue that the IMO only has a certain amount of bandwidth and
we should concentrate on decarbonisation and how to get to net zero by
2050."

He also noted [the [**major role** that **Russia played in**]{.mark}
**Arctic [shipping]{.mark}**]{.underline}. "[[Whether they would **abide
by the IMO's**]{.underline}]{.mark} [[**regulation** is **anyone's
guess**]{.mark} in the current circumstances]{.underline}," he said.

#### Japanese prolif is good\-\--increases regional stability and prevents further allied prolif.

**Corr 17** (Anders Corr, Principal at Corr Analytics---a political risk
analysis firm, former Team Leader of the Social Science Research and
Analysis Group of the U.S. Army's Human Terrain System, former United
States Pacific Command Intelligence Director at Booz Allen Hamilton,
holds a Ph.D. in Government from Harvard University and an M.A. in
International Relations from Yale University, 1-31-2017, "Japan: Go
Nuclear Now", Forbes,
https://www.forbes.com/sites/anderscorr/2017/01/31/japan-go-nuclear-now/#2618efe0566c,
accessed 7-9-2019)

[[If Japan goes nuclear, it will not cause non-nuclear]{.mark}
**authoritarian [countries]{.mark}**]{.underline} [**[to do the
same]{.underline}**]{.mark}*.* While some countries like China and North
Korea hype a threat from Japan in their state-run media, no serious
analysts see Japan as an aggressive power. It is a democracy with a
pacifist constitution. It has experienced the horrors of nuclear war as
has no other country. Vietnam, for example, will not see a Japanese
nuclear weapon as a threat. **[Rather, [it will see]{.mark} a [Japanese
nuclear weapon as stabilizing]{.mark} Asia through a check on
China,]{.underline}** Vietnam's main threat. Such a weapon could even
allow Japan to increase economic and military aid to Vietnam in the
safety of knowing that China would be less likely to retaliate.
[[Australia, So]{.mark}uth [Ko]{.mark}rea [and Taiwan might go nuclear,
but not from]{.mark} fear of [Japan]{.mark}. **In fact, [Japan]{.mark}'s
going nuclear might [decrease]{.mark} the [probability of]{.mark} these
[countries going nuclear because they]{.mark} would [feel safer]{.mark}
with Japan's new nuclear check on China. [If they did go
nuclear]{.mark}, however, [they would be]{.mark} additional [bulwarks
against]{.mark} authoritarian [aggression]{.mark} in Asia [and]{.mark}
thereby [increase stability]{.mark} in the region**]{.underline}. They
are well-developed democracies that would maintain security procedures
such that nuclear weapons did not fall into the hands of extremists.
[[As responsible democracies]{.mark}, they can be counted on to only
maintain their nuclear deterrent for defensive purposes.]{.underline}
China shows little regard for human rights in its own country. One
Chinese general threatened hundreds of U.S. cities with destruction
through nuclear war. China is a territorially aggressive power. And as a
result, countries near and far are increasingly worried. Only by
matching China's nuclear weapons will Asian democracies be able to
defend themselves. Ringing China with nuclear democracies will force it
to focus on more productive pursuits than threats and the violent
acquisition of its neighbors' territory.

#### Strong Sino-Russian relations cause war 

**Brands 25**, PhD History, Professor @ the Johns Hopkins University
School of Advanced International Studies. (Hal, 1-9-2025, \"The looming
Eurasian menace,\" The Washington Post, <https://archive.ph/C6mna>)

**[[China and Russia announced their "no limits" strategic
partnership]{.underline}]{.mark}** just before Moscow launched its
full-scale invasion of Ukraine. They mean, in Chinese President Xi
Jinping's words, [[to **revolutionize the international system**]{.mark}
by causing]{.underline} "[[changes the likes of which we haven't seen
for 100 years.]{.mark}" Since that invasion, Russia and North Korea have
sealed a formal military alliance]{.underline}; Russia and Iran have
built a stronger defense partnership in which technology, weapons and
know-how flow both ways. And since Oct. 7, 2023, [Russia has provided
targeting data and other assistance to the Houthis in Yemen, who have
also acquired missiles and other means of mayhem from
Tehran]{.underline}. Most of these relationships aren't new, but all
have grown rapidly in the hothouse global environment created by wars in
Ukraine and the Middle East. To be sure, there is no overarching
multilateral alliance --- no autocrats' NATO. Mistrust among the
illiberal powers remains pervasive. But their relationships constitute a
cohering network of ties among states that are determined to wreck Pax
Americana and command some of the most valuable strategic real estate on
Earth. Western observers play down the autocrats' pacts at their peril,
because some of history's greatest cataclysms have been caused by
dysfunctional --- yet profoundly destructive --- alliances. Nazi
Germany, Fascist Italy and Imperial Japan never could have coexisted in
the long run. But before World War II, their cooperation delivered
devastating multiplier effects by destabilizing governments on several
fronts at once. During the early Cold War, a wary alliance between
Moscow and Beijing intensified the threats facing the free world. And
whether the new autocratic pacts are love matches or marriages of
convenience, they are having serious strategic effects. (Juanjo Gasull
for The Washington Post) For one thing, these relationships encourage
aggression by reducing its costs: A heavily sanctioned Russia has been
able to continue its war in Ukraine thanks to the missiles, artillery
shells, drones and troops it has received from Tehran and Pyongyang, and
the economic succor it has gotten from Beijing. Likewise, the pacts
increase the danger of war on some fronts by pacifying others: Putin can
go all-out against Ukraine because he faces no threat from China, just
as Xi can be bolder in the Pacific because he has a friendly Russia at
his back. An autocratic peace within Eurasia encourages violent meddling
around its margins. Autocratic relationships also weaken Western
pressure on individual regimes: Russia now shields Pyongyang from
sanctions, while Tehran and Moscow develop illicit trade networks beyond
U.S. reach. [[These relationships divide U.S. attention by creating
trouble in several places]{.mark} at once]{.underline}. Witness
Washington's agonizing debates over aid for Ukraine vs. [support for
Taiwan](https://archive.ph/o/C6mna/https:/www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2023/05/18/taiwan-ukraine-support-russia-china/),
over [hammering the
Houthis](https://archive.ph/o/C6mna/https:/www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2024/01/16/red-sea-houthis-supply-chain-disruption/)
vs. husbanding missiles for a war with China. Perhaps most
fundamentally, autocratic ties are shifting the military balance.
Beijing's record-breaking buildup has long involved purchases of Russian
planes, missiles and air defenses. And [**[Sino-Russian defense
cooperation is only getting deeper]{.mark}** and more secretive as the
[two countries work together to build submarines, helicopters and other
key capabilities]{.mark}.]{.underline} Across Eurasia, in fact,
transnational production networks are emerging: Iranian-origin drones
are being built in Russian defense plants, where North Korean workers
reportedly labor. The autocratic arms trade is burgeoning, as Russia
promises to sell state-of-the-art aircraft, air defenses and other
advanced capabilities to its partners. Disruptive military innovation is
accelerating, as North Korea receives Russian technology and know-how
for its missile and satellite programs. These countries' militaries are
also learning from one another: Iran's [missile and drone
attack](https://archive.ph/o/C6mna/https:/mwi.westpoint.edu/what-irans-drone-attack-portends-for-the-future-of-warfare/)
against Israel in April looked suspiciously similar to Russia's previous
strikes against Ukraine. If the United States once served as the arsenal
of democracy, a modern-day arsenal of autocracy is now taking shape.
Russia, Iran, China and North Korea have already managed more
defense-technological collaboration than [the Axis
powers](https://archive.ph/o/C6mna/https:/www.nationalww2museum.org/war/articles/axis-powers-world-war-ii)
of the World War II era ever did. Even if they never come together in a
full-fledged military alliance, they could still deepen their
cooperation in dangerous ways. For example, they could collaborate on
more sensitive projects: Russia could support North Korea's rapidly
improving nuclear program or give Beijing its most advanced
submarine-quieting technology. Putin could give the Houthis anti-ship
cruise missiles to make their attacks more lethal. [[Russia and China
could swap]{.mark} [insights on how to use nuclear threats to keep the
United States from interfering as they]{.mark} coerce or [attack their
neighbors]{.mark} --- precisely what Putin has done in
Ukraine]{.underline}. Or perhaps [[these countries could find **more
dramatic ways to cooperate** in]{.mark} crisis or [conflict]{.mark}.
[China and Russia are]{.mark} [already conducting military drills in
potential conflict zones]{.mark} from the Baltic Sea to the Western
Pacific, [signaling]{.mark} [that **one illiberal power won't sit on the
sidelines as Washington fights another**]{.mark}. Russia could aid China
in a future conflict against the United States]{.underline} by
conducting cyberattacks that make it harder for America to mobilize. Or
Putin could menace Europe by posturing his forces there while the
Pentagon is preoccupied with a crisis in Asia. If any of this seems
unlikely, just remember that [[Western observers have repeatedly been
surprised]{.mark}, even shocked, [by how far these relationships have
already come]{.mark}.]{.underline} Meanwhile, closer ties among U.S.
adversaries raise the odds that a war that starts in one place will
spill into others. Recall that World War II began not as a global
conflict but as a set of regional crises that gradually intensified and
became inseparable --- just as the United States' Eurasian crises are
becoming sharper, and more interwoven, today. [The [U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates [is still struggling to adapt to this situation.
Washington continues to see the world as being divided]{.mark} [into
separate regions]{.mark}. This assumption is dangerously outdated. As
the Eurasian axis coheres, individual crises take on global meaning. It
might be fashionable to talk about pivoting away from Europe to focus on
China]{.underline}. But one reason it would be so damaging if America
abandons Ukraine, and Russia defeats it, is that this would be a victory
for the larger autocratic coalition in its showdown with the democratic
world.

### 1NC \-\-- Shell \-\-- Science

#### Plan: The United States federal government should enter prior, binding consultation with the Arctic Council over balancing \[[plan area]{.mark}\].

#### Proposing the plan to the arctic council is key to rebuild relations with Russia and ensures the council's revitalization.

Carol **Dyck,** 20**24** -- doctoral student Western University, Faculty
of Law "On thin ice: The Arctic Council's uncertain future" Marine
Policy Volume 163, May 2024,
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0308597X24000587> //
adam

Since 1996, the [[Arctic Council]{.underline}]{.mark} has [[served as a
critical forum]{.mark} through [which Arctic States]{.mark}]{.underline}
[[have collaborated]{.underline}]{.mark} [[on common
concerns]{.underline}]{.mark} affecting the North. The [consensus-based
structure]{.underline} of the Council, set apart from high politics,
[created the conditions for continued peaceful relations even as
tensions flared far from the Arctic Circle]{.underline}. [With the
Russian invasion of Ukraine and]{.underline} the [subsequent pause in
Arctic Council activities, the future of this "high level forum" came
into question]{.underline}. The ongoing Ukraine War threatens to unravel
the goodwill of the past quarter century, potentially undercutting the
Arctic Council as the preeminent channel for Arctic governance at a time
when environmental and geopolitical stressors necessitate greater
cooperation, not less. As [[a now isolated]{.mark}
[Russia]{.mark}]{.underline} [[seeks new partnerships]{.mark} [with
"friendly" States]{.mark} for Arctic missions, the **[threat of an
Arctic divided between East and West grows]{.mark}**]{.underline}. With
accelerating loss of sea-ice due to climate change, opening up the
Arctic to greater resource exploitation and shipping, animosity in the
North will further threaten this fragile ecosystem. This paper proposes
a return to the founding ethos of cooperation through science diplomacy
to rebuild strained relationships and prevent further fracturing of the
Arctic Council.

1\. Introduction

Since 1996, the Arctic Council has served as the conduit through which
Arctic issues are largely handled. While the Council\'s focus lies in
environmental protection and sustainable development, the dialogue that
it has facilitated between the Arctic States has contributed to ongoing
peaceful relations in the region during the post-Cold War period. Even
as East-West tensions rose during the Crimea conflict in 2014 and the
Syrian civil war, the Arctic States continued to collaborate on research
projects and efforts to create regulations and standards on fishing,
shipping, and other key issues. Indeed, the Council's success at
facilitating peaceful dialogue has earned it multiple nominations for a
Nobel Peace Prize, most recently in February 2022.

Shortly after that nomination, a crisis far from the Arctic threatened
the future of the Arctic Council. When Russia invaded Ukraine in
February 2022, the seven remaining Arctic States announced an immediate
pause of all Arctic Council work. Months passed with only limited
operations.1 The spillover of the Ukraine war to the Arctic has raised
several urgent questions regarding the future of the Arctic Council and
Arctic governance in general. It remains unclear when, or even if,
relations can resume normally after this most recent crisis, as Senior
Arctic Officials and others take stock of the situation and assess the
best path forward. Even before February 2022, tension between Arctic and
non-Arctic States had been building as the latter sought greater
influence over Arctic policy-making. Now, the strained geopolitical
environment along with ecological stressors put into strong relief
weaknesses of the Arctic Council that limit its ability to maintain
peace, manage emerging industries and satisfy demands for more inclusive
Arctic governance.

The Council's future is precarious; its absence would leave a
significant void, with far reaching consequences for Northern
inhabitants, and the regional and global environment. This paper will
contend that, to move forward, the Arctic States and other stakeholders
need to return to the core principles of the Arctic Council and its
predecessor - the Arctic Environmental Protection Strategy - to rebuild
trust and cooperation. Science diplomacy could serve as the catalyst to
bring the Arctic States together again, with a shared goal of increasing
knowledge of Arctic systems and species, and protecting this fragile
environment before intensifying stressors cause irreparable damage to
the region.

2\. History of the Arctic Council: science, diplomacy and cooperation

Since the end of the Cold War, narratives of the Arctic have highlighted
"Arctic exceptionalism". Despite armed conflicts, rising tensions and
jockeying for political and economic power prevalent in other areas of
the globe, the Arctic remained largely unaffected. This vision of a
peaceful Arctic is relatively new. During the Cold War, military
alliances carved up the Arctic, with security infrastructure
strategically placed in the territories of coastal Arctic States.
Demilitarization and interstate cooperation were unthinkable. Apart from
the collaborative success of the 1973 Agreement on the Conservation of
Polar Bears, "the members of the two alliance systems stared warily
across the pole at one another and saw only adversaries rather than
potential partners."2; Mistrust between the Arctic States, especially
the United States and the Soviet Union, pervaded Arctic politics.

When former President of the Soviet Union Mikhail Gorbachev came to
power in 1985, he sought to establish a détente with the West. In 1987,
as NATO States were building up their military infrastructure in the
Arctic, he delivered a pivotal speech in Murmansk, where he declared,
"Let the North of the globe, the Arctic, become a zone of peace".3 He
noted that "scientific exploration of the Arctic is of immense
importance for the whole of mankind", and called on Arctic States to
come together to create a plan to protect Arctic lands and waters.
Gorbachev hoped to expand collaborative research efforts and knowledge
sharing by building on the scientific exchange already occurring between
Russia and Canada.4 Gorbachev's speech was the initial step towards
abandoning entrenched Cold War suspicion-based policies to advance
regional governance in the Arctic. Two years later, on a formal visit to
the Soviet Union, former Canadian Prime Minister Brian Mulroney
celebrated the success of the Canadian-Soviet research partnership and
expressed hope that "this nascent multilateral environmental and
scientific cooperation become more broadly based to cover the full range
of economic and social issues,"5; demonstrating how science diplomacy
could lead to work in wider areas of mutual benefit.

During this time, Finland also raised concerns about air, water and soil
pollution originating from the Soviet Union. The 1986 Chernobyl disaster
reaffirmed these fears and brought the Arctic States together to address
transboundary pollution and contamination in the North. Efforts to
establish an environmental monitoring regime for the Arctic began in
1989, culminating in the 1991 Ministerial Declaration in Rovaniemi,
Finland, which created the Arctic Environmental Protection Strategy
(AEPS).6 However, Canada, following on Mulroney's Leningrad speech,
lobbied the Arctic States to create an Arctic organization that would go
beyond science and knowledge exchange to address economic concerns and
demilitarization of the Arctic. Canada sought an organization that would
have an autonomous chair, secretariat, and budget. Despite the end of
the Cold War, the United States pushed back against Canada's proposal
which ran counter to its military-oriented approach to the Arctic.7
Having just agreed to the AEPS, the American government saw no benefit
in another Arctic organization, with a permanent headquarters and staff
and a wider mandate beyond scientific research. In the end, significant
concessions were made to ensure American involvement in the Arctic
Council.

The resulting Arctic Council differed greatly from Canada's original
vision. The Council arose not from a treaty but rather a Ministerial
Declaration signed in Ottawa - the Ottawa Declaration - on September 19,
1996.8 As a "high level forum" designed to further "cooperation,
coordination and interaction among Arctic States",9 the Council may not
issue binding regulations. This format fit with the American idea that
the Council would periodically meet to share ideas, but not issue formal
decisions.10 Most notably, the Ottawa Declaration specifically states
that "\[t\]he Arctic Council should not deal with matters related to
military security."11; Consequently, the Council engages exclusively in
soft power issues tied to environmental protection and sustainable
development.12 Critically, science continued to be the unifying core of
the Arctic Council's existence.

The Council's inability to propose more than non-binding recommendations
in fact boosted its capacity to successfully influence the creation of
international law. Its less formal setting allowed a variety of
stakeholders to convene, without the constraints of high-level
diplomacy, to delve into topics of common concern and "hammer out the
terms of agreements".13 By channeling its efforts into cooperative
projects to produce high caliber environmental assessments, the Council
could alert the global community to emerging issues and thus initiate
policy action. Indeed, its production of quality scientific reports is
widely viewed as its most effective work.14 Over the past decade, as the
Council highlighted the challenges facing a rapidly changing Arctic, it
has been instrumental in furthering policies to regulate activities in
the marine environment. The Nuuk Declaration from the Seventh
Ministerial Meeting of the Arctic Council in May 2011 celebrated the
first legally binding agreement "under the auspices" of the Arctic
Council - the Agreement on Cooperation in Aeronautical and Maritime
Search and Rescue in the Arctic (Arctic SAR Agreement).15 In addition,
the Arctic Council played a central role in the 2013 Agreement on Arctic
Marine Oil Pollution Preparedness and Response, while the Protection of
the Arctic Marine Environment (PAME) Working Group's 2009 Arctic Marine
Shipping Assessment contributed to the creation of the International
Maritime Organization's 2017 International Code for Ships Operating in
Polar Waters (Polar Code).16 Thus, though the Arctic Council possesses
limited authority, over the course of its existence, this
consensus-based organization has evolved to become the preeminent actor
in Arctic issues and demonstrated its ability to influence Arctic
governance on a grand scale.

3\. The temporary suspension of Arctic Council operations

Since the inception of the Arctic Council, the Arctic states have
succeeded in separating global tensions from Arctic affairs to maintain
peace and cooperation in the North.17 Even following the 2014 Crimea
Crisis and Syria's civil war, as relations between Russia and the other
Arctic States disintegrated, Arctic cooperation not only continued
unaffected, but the Arctic States were able to come together to create
an unprecedented precautionary-based agreement with the 2018 Central
Arctic Ocean Fisheries Agreement. Still, Arctic Council insiders warned
that tense relations between Russia and the United States, as well as
distrust between Arctic and non-Arctic States, could erode the Arctic
Council\'s effectiveness.18 In 2019, for the first time, the Arctic
States failed to issue a Ministerial Declaration due to the United
States' refusal under former President Donald Trump's Presidency to
permit the term "climate change" in the final document.19 The day before
the Ministerial Meeting, former US Secretary of State Mike Pompeo called
out China for its "words and actions" and criticized Russia for its
"aggressive behaviour" in the Arctic.20 Given the timing and venue of
Pompeo's speech, it seemed inevitable that Arctic and non-Arctic States
could no longer compartmentalize the work of the Council as they strive
to assert dominance in wider political and economic spheres.

Despite the growing tension, at the beginning of February 2022, members
of Norway's Parliament nominated the Arctic Council for the Nobel Peace
Prize. Member of Parliament Bård Ludvig Thorheim stated,

We believe \[the Arctic Council's\] cooperation is exceptional in
international politics and demonstrates the need for cooperation and
trust between countries in a time where peace is threatened around
Ukraine and other regions.21

Weeks later, Russia invaded Ukraine, an act which violated Article 2(4)
of the UN Charter, prohibiting Members States from "the threat or use of
force against the territorial integrity or political independence of any
State."22; In response to this breach of international law, the
remaining Arctic Council Member States issued a joint declaration to
announce that they would temporarily pause "participation in all
meetings of the Council and its subsidiary bodies",23, 25 affecting 128
ongoing projects involving scientific cooperation and outreach.24 They
further stated,

We remain convinced of the enduring value of the Arctic Council for
circumpolar cooperation and reiterate our support for this institution
and its work.

We hold a responsibility to the people of the Arctic, including the
indigenous peoples of the Arctic, who contribute to and benefit from the
important work undertaken in the Council.25

The Ukraine war demonstrated how "interconnected, interdependent, and
inherently linked the peoples, governments, resources, and landscapes of
Arctic nations are with others around the globe."26 With the pause in
Council operations, the era of "Arctic exceptionalism" passed.

The future of the Arctic Council now hangs in the balance. The ongoing
Ukraine war has triggered renewed military posturing in the Arctic
Ocean, further souring relations between the Member States.27 In May
2022 Senior Arctic Officials (SAOs) continued to search for solutions to
the two month pause, a situation complicated by Russia holding the
Chairmanship. By June 2022 the Arctic seven announced that they would
"implement a limited resumption of \[their\] work in the Arctic Council
on projects that do not involve the participation of the Russian
Federation" and would "continue to examine additional modalities to
allow \[them\] to further continue the council's important work."28;
Meanwhile, academics likewise explored whether the remaining seven
Member States could establish a new Arctic organization to address
cooperation in the region without Russia.29 The American coordinator for
the Arctic region, James P. Deltart, of the US State Department rejected
that option stating,

Creating an alternative structure to the Arctic Council is not on our
agenda. We believe the Council holds the greatest value as a circumpolar
forum including all the eight Arctic states and binding together the
people who live throughout the entire region.30

Senior Arctic Officials remain wary of making rash decisions which may
prevent a future return to normalcy in the Arctic Council. To abandon
the Arctic Council altogether would permanently isolate Russia, an
imprudent move given that Russia is, from "a variety of biophysical and
socioeconomic perspectives \[...\], the preeminent Arctic State."31; A
new regional Arctic body that does not include Russia would lack scope
and breadth, threatening its ability to fulfill its goals or objectives.

Furthermore, China made clear in October 2022 that it would not
acknowledge an Arctic Council that does not include Russia.32 At the
time, the Chinese Special Envoy to the Arctic, Feng Gao, stated at the
Arctic Circle Assembly that,

The Arctic Council is based on a declaration and there is no procedure
for leaving the Council. I doubt that the Chairmanship can be
transferred to anyone or that Norway can take over the Chair without
Russia from a legal point of view.33

If the remaining seven Member States go forward with a different
institution, it is unclear whether China would recognize its
legitimacy.34 China may also decline to participate as an Observer in
the Arctic Council if Russia is barred from meetings and collaborative
research projects. At the same time, it appreciates that it must not
adversely affect its relationships with the other Arctic Council Member
States.35 Given that China is proving itself a significant and
formidable player in the Arctic, its absence from the Arctic Council, or
a new regional forum without Russia, could undermine efforts to address
Arctic shipping, fishing, and other economic and environmental concerns.
China may see this current crisis as an opportunity to double down on
its efforts to open governance to the international community and
increase its influence.

On May 11, 2023 Norway assumed Chairship36 of the Arctic Council
following a sparsely-attended ceremony which took place both in
Salekhard, Russia and online. Though the Meeting ran relatively smoothly
following months of negotiations between Russia and Norway, it was the
first instance that the transfer of Chair was not through a
"Ministerial" meeting.37 When Norway took over as Chair, the
long-present note on the Arctic Council's website which read, "The
Arctic Council is pausing all official meetings of the Council and its
subsidiary bodies until further notice" was removed.38 However, at the
time of writing, political meetings at the high level cannot take place,
nor are SAO meetings planned for the immediate future.39 Norway has a
difficult task ahead, attempting to bring the Arctic States together and
working towards the goal of a fully functioning Arctic Council. On the
surface, all Arctic States assert that they wish to keep the Arctic as a
"territory of peace, stability and mutually beneficial cooperation to
ensure its sustainable and safe development", as voiced by the Russian
Ministry of Foreign Affairs,40 but mistrust and suspicion remain
pervasive, driving a wedge between the Member States as well as between
the Observers.

The [pause in Arctic Council]{.underline} [projects]{.underline}
